Causality and Created Things

Two Possibilities for How Divine and Human Powers Cooperate
Two possibilities for how divine and human powers cooperate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two ways scholars have explained how both divine power and human power can affect a single action.

Core Idea
Two powers can affect one act: either both independently, or human power through divine aid.

In the previous lesson, we saw one opinion that only divine power affects actions. Now we encounter a second opinion that both powers are involved. But how exactly do they combine? Two possibilities are offered, each with different implications for divine sovereignty and human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the Second Opinion
Start by reading the passage that states the second opinion: both divine and human powers affect the act. Notice that it is introduced as an alternative to the first opinion.
2
Identify the First Possibility
The first possibility claims that both powers are independent effectors and that their coexistence on one effect is permissible. Consider what this means for causality.
3
Identify the Second Possibility
The second possibility distinguishes degrees of independence: divine power is independent, human power becomes independent only through divine aid. Note the term 'i'ānah (aid).
4
Consider the Attribution to Abu Ishaq
The passage notes that this second view is attributed to Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, but with a qualification. Reflect on why such a qualification might be necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول الثاني : إن المؤثر في وجود ذلك الفعل هو قدرة الله تعالى ، مع قدرة العبد · ثم ههنا احتمالان : أحدهما : أن يقال : إن قدرة الله تعالى مستقلة بالتأثير [ وقدرة العبد أيضا مستقلة بالتأثير (۳) ] إلا أن اجتماع المؤثرين المستقلين على الأثر الواحد : جائز (1) والثاني : أن يقال : قدرة الله تعالى مستقلة بالتأثير ، وقدرة العبد غير مستقلة بالتأثير، وإذا انضمت قدرة الله تعالى إلى قدرة العبد ، صارت قدرة العبد مستقلة بالتأثير بتوسط هذه الإعانة. ويقال إن هذا القول هو مذهب الأستاذ ( أبي إسحاق ، إلا أنه يحكى عنه أنه قال : قدرة العبد تؤثر بمعنى ) .
The second statement: That which effects the existence of that act is the power of Allah the Exalted along with the power of the servant. Then here are two possibilities: One of them: That it be said: The power of Allah the Exalted is independent in effect, and the power of the servant is also independent in effect, except that the coming together of two independent effectors on one effect is permissible. The second: That it be said: The power of Allah the Exalted is independent in effect, and the power of the servant is not independent in effect, and when the power of Allah the Exalted joins with the power of the servant, the power of the servant becomes independent in effect by means of this aid. And it is said that this statement is the doctrine of al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq, except that it is reported from him that he said: The power of the servant effects in a sense (bi ma'na).
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage presents the second major opinion regarding the influence of power on the action: that both divine power and human power affect the existence of the act. Two sub-possibilities are given. The first possibility states that both powers are independent effectors (mu'aththiran mustaqillan), and their simultaneous application to a single effect is permissible. The second possibility holds that divine power is independent, but human power is not; however, when divine power assists it, human power becomes independent through that aid. This second view is attributed to Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, though it is reported that he used the phrase 'affects in a sense' (tu'aththir bi ma'na), perhaps indicating a qualified form of causation.

Key Terms

قدرة العبد
اجتماع المؤثرين
إعانة
تأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two independent powers cannot affect the same effect.
Correction: The first possibility explicitly states that the coexistence of two independent effectors on one effect is permissible (jā'iz).

Checkpoint
In the second possibility of the second opinion, what is needed for human power to become independent in effect?
A) It is always independent
B) It requires the joining of divine power as aid
C) It becomes independent only when the action is a sin
D) It is never independent

Key Takeaway
The second opinion on divine and human causation offers two ways to reconcile both powers: either both are independent effectors, or human power requires divine aid to become independent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the two possibilities again carefully. Which one describes how you truly understand your own actions—do you feel that you act independently of Allah, or that your ability to act is entirely dependent on His aid? Be honest: in your daily life, do you live as if you are an independent effector, or as if you are constantly sustained by divine assistance?
?
The first possibility states that two independent effectors can coexist on one effect. How does this challenge your assumption about how much control you really have over your deeds? If both you and Allah are independent effectors of your actions, what does that imply about your responsibility and your dependence on Him?
?
Abu Ishaq said the servant's power 'effects in a sense.' What does this qualification reveal about the nature of your own causality? Are you comfortable with the idea that your power is only real in a qualified, dependent sense? How should this reshape your reliance on Allah in every action, from the smallest to the greatest?

The Third Opinion: Action from Power and Motive Combined
The author states the third opinion and divides its supporters on causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third opinion's claim that action necessarily follows the combination of power and motive, and the major disagreements among its proponents regarding the role of that combination in causation.

Core Idea
Action becomes necessary when power and motive combine, but who causes existence is debated.

This passage presents the author's chosen position on the cause of action. He argues that a capable agent, equally able to act or refrain, only acts when a motive tips the balance. The passage then explores two major interpretations of this causal process, one involving necessary emanation and the other involving free choice, though the latter is cut short.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Note the key terms and the logical structure of the argument.
The passage ends abruptly; focus on what is present.
2
Understand the preponderance argument
The author argues that without a motive, power remains indifferent (capacity to do or refrain). The motive creates preponderance (rujhan), making the act necessary. Trace this reasoning step by step.
3
Identify the two main subgroups
Proponents of the third opinion disagree on whether the combination of power and motive is the direct cause of the act's existence. The passage name two broader groups and then subdivides the second group into philosophers and free-chooser theologians.
The free-chooser view is incomplete due to page break.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول الثالث : إن حصول الفعل عقيب مجموع القدرة مع الداعي واجب . وذلك لأن القادر من حيث إنه قادر يمكنه الفعل بدلا عن الترك ، وبالعكس. ومع حصول هذا الاستواء ، يمتنع رجحان أحد الطرفين على الآخر . فإذا انضاف إليها(۱) حصول الداعي : حصل رجحان جانب الوجود . وعند ذلك يصير الفعل واجب الوقوع . وهذا القول هو المختار عندنا · ثم القائلون بهذا القول . إما أن يقولوا : المؤثر في دخول الفعل في الوجود : مجموع القدرة مع الداعي، وإما أن يقولوا : ليس المؤثر في دخول الفعل في الوجود هو مجموع القدرة والداعي . وهؤلاء أيضا فريقان : فأما الذين زعموا : أن مدير [ هذا (۲) ] العالم موجب بالذات . قالوا : إن عند حصول القدرة مع الداعي يحصل الاستعداد التام ، لدخول ذلك الفعل في الوجود إلا أن هذه القوى الجسمانية ليست لها صلاحية الإيجاد والتأثير فعند حصول الاستعداد التام يفيض الوجـود من واهب الصور ، على تلك الماهيات وتصير موجودة . فحصول القدرة والداعي يفيد الاستعداد التام . وأما الوجود والحصول ، فذاك من واهب الصور . وهذا مذهب جمهور الفلاسفة . وأما الذين زعموا : أن مدير هذا العالم فاعل مختار . قالوا : إن مجموع
And the third statement: That the occurrence of the act subsequent to the combination of power and motive is necessary. That is because the capable agent (al-qadir) insofar as he is capable, he can do the act instead of refraining, and vice versa. And with this equivalence, preponderance of one side over the other is impossible. So when the occurrence of the motive is added to it, a preponderance of the side of existence occurs. And at that point the act becomes necessary of occurrence. And this statement is the chosen one for us. Then those who say this statement: some of them say: The effector (al-mu'aththir) in bringing the act into existence is the combination of power and motive. And some of them say: The effector in bringing the act into existence is not the combination of power and motive. And these are also two groups: As for those who claim that the manager of this world is necessary by essence (mujib bi al-dhat), they said: When power along with motive is present, complete readiness (al-isti'dad al-tamm) comes about for the entrance of that act into existence, except that these bodily powers do not have the capability of bringing into existence and effect. So when complete readiness occurs, existence emanates from the Bestower of Forms (Wahib al-Suwar) upon those essences (mahiyyat) and they become existent. So the occurrence of power and motive yields complete readiness. As for existence and occurrence, that is from the Bestower of Forms. This is the doctrine of the majority of philosophers. And as for those who claim that the manager of this world is a free chooser (fa'il mukhtar), they said: That the combination...
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents the third opinion, which the author identifies as his chosen view. It states that the occurrence of an act necessarily follows the combination of power (qudra) and motive (da'i). The reasoning is that a capable agent, considered as such, can equally do or refrain from an action. Without a preponderance, neither possible would occur. When the motive is added, a preponderance (rujhan) for existence arises, making the act necessary. The passage then divides the proponents of this view into two groups regarding whether the combination itself is the cause of the act's existence. The first subgroup includes those who say the combination is the cause. The second subgroup includes those who deny that, and they split further into: (a) those who consider the world's manager as necessary by essence (the philosophers), who hold that the combination provides complete readiness, and then existence emanates from the Bestower of Forms; (b) those who consider the manager as a free chooser (the theologians), whose view is cut off at the end of the page, stating 'they said: That the combination...'

Key Terms
قدرة
داعي
رجحان
استعداد تام
واهب الصور
فاعل مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third opinion is that human power alone causes action.
Correction: The third opinion requires both power and motive; power alone is insufficient as it is equally capable of act or refraining.

Checkpoint
According to the third opinion, what makes the act necessary of occurrence?
A) The presence of power alone
B) The presence of motive alone
C) The combination of power and motive
D) The absence of any obstacle

Key Takeaway
The third opinion holds that the combination of power and motive makes the act necessary, but proponents disagree on whether this combination directly causes existence or merely prepares readiness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as though your actions are necessary given your power and motives, or do you truly believe you could choose otherwise? If the act becomes necessary when power and motive combine, how do you account for the moments when you had both but still refrained? What does that reveal about your deepest motives?
?
The philosophers say existence comes only from the Bestower of Forms, not from your own combination of power and motive. Does that relieve you of responsibility or make you more accountable? How would your sense of sin and repentance change if you believed your actions are merely readiness for God’s direct creation?
?
When you sin, do you tend to think, 'I couldn't help it; the motive was too strong'? If so, this passage asserts that the act becomes necessary, but it also implies that the motive itself is within your purview. Will you examine your motives today and ask yourself whether you are cultivating motives that lead to righteousness or rebellion?

Divine Independence and Human Dependence
Razi presents the second view on causal power: divine independent, human dependent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second view on causality: that divine power is inherently independent, while human power gains conditional independence through divine assistance.

Core Idea
Human power is only effective when joined by divine aid.

This lesson introduces a key theological position on how divine and human power interact in causing actions. The passage from Razi outlines the view that God's power is always independent, whereas human power becomes independent only when God's power joins it. This view carefully balances divine sovereignty with human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: قدرة (power), استقلال (independence), إعانة (aid).
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the two claims
The passage makes two main claims: (1) God's power is independent in effect, (2) human power is not independent, but becomes independent through divine aid. Find these in the text.
Then note the attribution to Abu Ishaq with a qualification.
3
Reflect on the qualification
Consider why Abu Ishaq's reported statement that human power 'has effect in a sense' is important. It shows the view does not grant full independence to humans.
This guards against misunderstanding human power as truly independent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : أن يقال : قدرة الله تعالى مستقلة بالتأثير ، وقدرة العبد غير مستقلة بالتأثير، وإذا انضمت قدرة الله تعالى إلى قدرة العبد ، صارت قدرة العبد مستقلة بالتأثير بتوسط هذه الإعانة. ويقال إن هذا القول هو مذهب الأستاذ ( أبي إسحق ، إلا أنه يحكى عنه أنه قال : قدرة العبد تؤثر بمعنى ) .
The second: that it is said: the power of God Almighty is independent in effect, and the power of the servant is not independent in effect. When the power of God Almighty joins the power of the servant, the power of the servant becomes independent in effect through the mediation of this aid. And it is said that this view is the school of the master (Abi Ishaq, except that it is narrated from him that he said: the power of the servant has effect in a sense).
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage defines the second theological view regarding the relationship between divine and human power in causing actions. It states that God's power (qudra) is independent (mustaqilla) in producing effects, whereas human power is not independent. However, when God's power joins human power through divine assistance (iʿāna), the human power becomes independent in effect by means of that aid. This view is attributed to Abu Ishaq, but it is also reported that he said human power 'has effect in a sense' (bi-maʿnā), implying a qualified, non-literal effect. The passage distinguishes between the independence of divine power and the conditional effectiveness of human power.

Key Terms
قدرة
استقلال
إعانة
تأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that human power becomes truly independent, as if it operates without need for God.
Correction: The text clarifies that human power becomes independent only 'through the mediation of this aid' (bi-tawassuṭ hādhihi al-iʿāna), meaning its independence is derived and conditional, not absolute.

Checkpoint
According to the second view presented, what happens when God's power joins the servant's power?
A) The servant's power becomes independent in effect through that aid.
B) The servant's power becomes completely independent without need for God.
C) God's power becomes dependent on the servant's power.
D) The servant's power is cancelled.

Key Takeaway
The second view holds that divine power is inherently independent, while human power gains conditional independence only through divine assistance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in an act of worship, do you genuinely feel that it is God's aid that made it possible, or do you secretly attribute it to your own strength? How would your daily practice change if you reminded yourself that your power is never truly independent without Him?
?
This lesson teaches that human power becomes effective only when joined by divine aid. Is there an area in your life where you are striving alone, relying on your own abilities, without consciously seeking God's assistance? What would it mean for you to invite His aid into that struggle starting today?
?
The passage distinguishes between independent divine power and dependent human power. Have you ever experienced a moment when your own efforts clearly fell short, and only through divine intervention did you succeed? How does that memory shape your understanding of your own agency and your dependence on God?

The Third View: Act Becomes Necessary with Power and Motive
Razi presents the third view and its philosophical subdivision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the act becomes necessary when power and motive combine, and how this view divides regarding the effective factor and the role of the Giver of Forms.

Core Idea
The act becomes inevitable only when both power and motive are present.

Razi presents the third of three views on causation of human acts. He argues that the act becomes necessary when power (qudra) and motive (dāʿī) come together. This view, which he endorses, then branches into two positions on what actually brings the act into existence. The passage also introduces the philosophical account involving the Giver of Forms (Wāhib al-Ṣuwar).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the argument and its flow.
Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'فإذا' and 'ثم'.
2
Identify the core claim
The passage begins by stating that the act becomes necessary (wājib) when power and motive combine. Note the reasoning: without motive, the agent's capability is evenly balanced, so the act cannot preponderate. The motive tips the scale.
Razi says this view is 'al-mukhtār ʿindanā' (the chosen one for us).
3
Trace the division among holders of this view
Razi then divides those who hold this view into two groups: (a) those who say the effective factor is the sum of power and motive, and (b) those who deny that. Among the latter, there is a subgroup: the philosophers who believe the world is governed by a necessary essence.
This is a classification tree.
4
Understand the philosophers' explanation
The philosophers argue that power and motive produce complete readiness (istiʿdād tāmm), but since bodily powers cannot create, existence emanates from the Giver of Forms (Wāhib al-Ṣuwar). So the readiness is from the sum, but existence is from God.
This is a key concept in Islamic philosophy (falsafa).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول الثالث : إن حصول الفعل عقيب مجموع القدرة مع الداعي واجب . وذلك لأن القادر من حيث إنه قادر يمكنه الفعل بدلا عن الترك ، وبالعكس. ومع حصول هذا الاستواء ، يمتنع رجحان أحد الطرفين على الآخر . فإذا انضاف إليها حصول الداعي : حصل رجحان جانب الوجود . وعند ذلك يصير الفعل واجب الوقوع . وهذا القول هو المختار عندنا . ثم القائلون بهذا القول . إما أن يقولوا : المؤثر في دخول الفعل في الوجود : مجموع القدرة مع الداعي، وإما أن يقولوا : ليس المؤثر في دخول الفعل في الوجود هو مجموع القدرة والداعي . وهؤلاء أيضا فريقان : فأما الذين زعموا : أن مدير [ هذا ] العالم موجب بالذات . قالوا : إن عند حصول القدرة مع الداعي يحصل الاستعداد التام ، لدخول ذلك الفعل في الوجود إلا أن هذه القوى الجسمانية ليست لها صلاحية الإيجاد والتأثير فعند حصول الاستعداد التام يفيض الوجود من واهب الصور ، على تلك الماهيات وتصير موجودة . فحصول القدرة والداعي يفيد الاستعداد التام . وأما الوجود والحصول ، فذاك من واهب الصور . وهذا مذهب جمهور الفلاسفة .
And the third view: that the occurrence of the act consequent upon the combination of power and motive is necessary. That is because the capable (qādir) insofar as he is capable can perform the act instead of omission, and vice versa. With this equality, preponderance of one side over the other is impossible. So when the occurrence of the motive is added to it, preponderance of the side of existence occurs. At that point the act becomes necessary of occurrence. This is the view chosen by us. Then those who hold this view: either they say that the effective factor in the entry of the act into existence is the combination of power and motive, or they say that the effective factor in the entry of the act into existence is not the combination of power and motive. And these are also two groups: as for those who claimed that the manager of this world is necessary by essence, they said: when power with motive is obtained, complete readiness (istiʿdād tāmm) occurs for the entry of that act into existence, but these bodily powers do not have the capacity for bringing about and effecting. So when complete readiness occurs, existence emanates from the Giver of Forms (Wāhib al-Ṣuwar) upon those essences, and they become existent. So the obtaining of power and motive yields complete readiness. As for existence and attainment, that is from the Giver of Forms. This is the school of the majority of philosophers.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents the third view regarding causation of human acts. It begins by stating that the act becomes necessary (wājib) when power (qudra) and motive (dāʿī) combine. The reasoning is that a capable agent (qādir) is equally capable of performing or omitting an act. With this equilibrium, neither side can preponderate. When a motive is added, the side of existence preponderates, making the act inevitable. The author says this is the chosen view (al-mukhtār ʿindanā). Then he divides those who hold this view into two groups: those who say the effective factor (muʾaththir) is the sum of power and motive, and those who say it is not. Among those who deny the sum as effective, there are two subgroups. The first subgroup are the philosophers who believe the world is governed by a necessary essence (mūjib bi-al-dhāt). They argue that the combination of power and motive produces complete readiness (istiʿdād tāmm) in the bodily powers, but since these powers cannot create, existence flows from the Giver of Forms (Wāhib al-Ṣuwar) upon the essences. Thus, the readiness is from the combination, but actual existence is from the Giver of Forms. This is the view of most philosophers.

Key Terms
قدرة
داعي
قادر
استعداد تام
واهب الصور
مختار
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the act becomes necessary only because of power, but the text emphasizes that without the motive (dāʿī) the equilibrium remains, and the act does not occur.
Correction: The necessity arises from the conjunction of both power and motive, not power alone.

Checkpoint
According to the third view, why does an act become necessary of occurrence?
A) Because power alone is sufficient.
B) Because the motive alone causes the act.
C) Because power and motive together produce preponderance of the side of existence.
D) Because the agent has no free will.

Key Takeaway
The conjunction of power and motive makes an act necessary, but for philosophers the actual existence comes from the Giver of Forms, not from human power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you consider the motive (dāʿī) that tips the balance? Is your reliance on your own power or on the Giver of Forms for its existence?
?
Reflect on a recent action you thought was entirely your doing. How does this lesson challenge the assumption that you are the sole cause? Can you see the role of divine giving even in your everyday choices?
?
The philosophers claim bodily powers only produce readiness; existence is from the Giver. Does this cause you to feel humble or anxious? How does it affect your responsibility for your actions?

Free Agent Causal Nexus: Power and Motive
Razi presents the second subgroup of the third view, which affirms a free agent and explains that the sum of power and motive necessitates the act by God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the free agent view explains that the sum of power and motive necessitates the act, while both are created by God's power alone.

Core Idea
Human power and motive necessitate the act, but God creates both the cause and the effect.

Razi continues distinguishing subgroups within the third view. Here we encounter those who affirm a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār) governing the world. They present a nuanced causal theory: the combination of power and motive does necessitate the act, but this entire causal nexus—both the necessitating condition and its result—exists only through God's creative power. An analogy with substance and accident clarifies their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the key terms 'fāʿil mukhtār', 'malzūm', 'lāzim'.
2
Identify the claim and its qualification
The passage first states the claim (sum of power and motive necessitates the act) and then immediately qualifies it (both cause and effect are by God's power). Mark these two parts.
3
Understand the analogy
The analogy with substance (jawhar) and accident (ʿaraḍ) shows how two things can be necessarily connected yet both are created by God. Explain the analogy in your own words.
Substance and accident are mutually dependent, but both are brought into existence by God.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that 'necessitation' removes divine involvement. How does the passage guard against this?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الذين زعموا : أن مدير هذا العالم فاعل مختار . قالوا : إن مجموع القدرة والداعي يستلزم حصول الفعل، إلا أن الملزوم واللازم إنما يحصلان بقدرة الله تعالى كما أن الجوهر والعرض متلازمان، ومع ذلك فإنهما لا يوجدان إلا بقدرة الله تعالى
And as for those who claimed that the manager of this world is a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār), they said: the sum of power and motive necessitates the occurrence of the act, except that the necessitating (malzūm) and the necessitated (lāzim) only occur by the power of God Almighty, just as substance and accident are mutually concomitant, and yet they do not exist except by the power of God Almighty.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents the second subgroup within the third view, namely those who affirm that the governor of the world is a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār) rather than a necessary essence. Their position: the combination of power and motive necessitates (yastalzim) the occurrence of the act. However, both the necessitating condition (malzūm) and the necessitated result (lāzim) are brought into existence only by God's power. The author draws an analogy: substance (jawhar) and accident (ʿaraḍ) are mutually concomitant (mutalāzimān) — one cannot exist without the other — yet both are created solely by God's power. Thus, although the act follows necessarily from the conjunction of power and motive, this entire causal nexus is sustained by God. This preserves divine omnipotence while acknowledging a genuine causal link between human preparation and the act.

Key Terms
فاعل مختار
ملزوم
لازم
جوهر
عرض
تلازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'necessitation' implies coercion or removes divine involvement.
Correction: The text clarifies that both the condition and its consequence are entirely dependent on God's power.

Checkpoint
According to the free agent interpretation of the third view, what is the relationship between the sum of power and motive and the act?
A) The sum of power and motive directly creates the act.
B) The sum necessitates the act, but both are created by God.
C) The sum has no causal role.
D) The act occurs by chance.

Key Takeaway
The free agent view allows for genuine necessitation by human power and motive, but insists that God creates the entire causal chain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan an action and feel confident in your ability to execute, do you recognize that both your power and your motive are created by God in that very moment? What would change if you truly internalized this?
?
Consider a recent decision you made where you felt your choice was free. Can you see how the 'sum of power and motive' was created by God, and yet you still experienced it as your own? How does this tension affect your relationship with divine reliance?
?
If every causal nexus is upheld by God's power, then even your failure to act when you had power and motive is also from God. How does this truth confront your pride in your successes and your despair over your failures?

The Mu'tazilite View of Independent Human Power and the Role of Motive
Razi presents the Mu'tazilite view of independent human power and the first internal dispute on the role of motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite claim that human power independently produces acts and their internal disagreement on whether this power depends on a motive.

Core Idea
Human power is independent, but Mu'tazilites disagree on whether it needs a motive to act.

The Mu'tazilites argued that humans create their own actions independently. However, they were not unanimous on how this power relates to motives. In this passage, Razi presents the core Mu'tazilite position and highlights a key internal dispute, particularly the inconsistency of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: مؤثر, استقلال, داعي.
2
Identify the main Mu'tazilite claim
What is the fourth view? The text says 'the effective factor is the power of the servant independently.' That is the core.
3
Understand the first dispute
The disagreement is about whether the effect of power depends on the existence of a motive (داعي). Abu al-Husayn gave contradictory statements.
See the commentary for explanation of his inconsistency.
4
Note the majority position
Most Mu'tazilite shaykhs do not make the act dependent on a motive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول الرابع : إن المؤثر في حصول الفعل هو قدرة العبد على سبيل الاستقلال . وهذا هو قول المعتزلة . ثم هؤلاء اختلفوا في مواضع : فالأول : إن تأثير القدرة في حصول الفعل. هل هو موقوف على حصول ؟ أما « أبو الحسين ، فقد اضطرب قوله فيه . فكلما تكلم مع الداعي في قولهم : لم خصص الله إحداث العالم بالوقت المعين ، دون ما قبله أو ما بعده ؟ قال : ( الفعل لا يتوقف على الداعي ، وكلما تكلم في سائر المسائل مع أصحابه . قال : و الفعل يتوقف على الداعي ، وأن الرجحان من غير المرجح : باطل في بدائه العقول ) . وأما مشايخ المعتزلة فأكثرهم [ لا يوقفون ] حصول الفعل على الداعي . ثم إذا قلنا : إن الفعل يتوقف على الداعي . فهل يصير الفعل واجب الوقوع عند حصول الداعية الخالصة ؟ فالأليق بكلام و أبي الحسين ، أنه يسلم الوجوب . وقال صاحبه ( محمود الخوارزمي ) : ( إن الفعل عند حصول الداعي يترقى من حد التساوي ، ولكن لا ينتهي إلى حد الوجوب ، بل يصير أولى بالوقوع » . والثاني : إن العلم بكون العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه : علم ضروري أو نظري ؟ فالجمهور من مشايخ المعتزلة اتفقوا على أنه علم نظري .
And the fourth view: that the effective factor in the occurrence of the act is the power of the servant independently. This is the statement of the Mu'tazilites. Then these differed on several points: the first: is the effect of power in the occurrence of the act dependent on [the existence of] the motive? As for Abu al-Husayn, his statement on this is inconsistent. Whenever he spoke about the motive in their saying: 'Why did God specify the creation of the world at a particular time rather than before or after it?' he said: 'The act is not dependent on the motive.' And whenever he spoke in other issues with his companions, he said: 'The act is dependent on the motive, and that preponderance without a preponderant is invalid in the beginnings of intellects.' As for the shaykhs of the Mu'tazilites, most of them do not make the occurrence of the act dependent on the motive. Then if we say that the act is dependent on the motive, does it become necessary of occurrence when the pure motive (dāʿiya khāliṣa) is obtained? The more consistent with the statements of Abu al-Husayn is that he concedes necessity. And his companion (Mahmud al-Khwarazmi) said: 'The act at the occurrence of the motive ascends from the level of equality, but does not reach the level of necessity; rather it becomes more deserving of occurrence.' The second: is the knowledge that the servant is the creator of his own acts necessary or theoretical? The majority of the shaykhs of the Mu'tazilites agreed that it is theoretical knowledge.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth view, that of the Mu'tazilites, who hold that the servant's power (qudra) is the independent (mustaqill) effective factor in producing the act. The author then details their internal disagreements on two points. The first dispute concerns whether the effect of power depends on the existence of a motive (dāʿī). Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's position is inconsistent: when arguing about why God created the world at a specific time, he denied that the act depends on a motive; but in other contexts he affirmed it, citing the principle that preponderance without a preponderant is rationally impossible. Most Mu'tazilite shaykhs, however, do not make the act dependent on a motive. The second dispute arises if we grant dependency on the motive: does the act become necessary (wājib) when the pure motive (dāʿiya khāliṣa) is present? Abu al-Husayn, according to the text, would concede necessity, but his associate Mahmud al-Khwarazmi says the act only becomes more deserving (awlā) but not necessary. The third point listed is about the epistemological status of our knowledge that the servant is the creator of his own acts: is it necessary (ḍarūrī) or theoretical (naẓarī)? Most Mu'tazilite scholars hold it to be theoretical, meaning it requires reasoning and is not self-evident.

Key Terms
مؤثر
استقلال
داعي
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think all Mu'tazilites held that the act is entirely independent of any motive.
Correction: The text shows that at least some, like Abu al-Husayn, sometimes affirmed dependency, and others like the majority denied it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the core Mu'tazilite view of human power?
A) Human power is dependent on God's power
B) Human power is the independent effective factor in the act
C) Human power is irrelevant
D) Human power is created by God but effective

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites affirm human independence, but disagree on whether the act depends on a motive, with Abu al-Husayn giving contradictory statements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own actions, do you believe they are fully independent, or do you sense a need for a motive or cause? How does this affect your understanding of your accountability before God? Commit to examining one action this week where you felt completely autonomous—was it truly without any influencing motive?
?
The passage shows Abu al-Husayn contradicting himself depending on the context. Are there areas in your own faith or worldview where you hold inconsistent positions without realizing it? Identify one such inconsistency and decide today to resolve it by seeking truth from the sources.
?
If human power were truly independent of any motive, what would that mean for your reliance on God? Would you still feel the need to pray for guidance or strength? Reflect on a moment when you acted without seeking God's help—what was the outcome? Will you change that habit starting now?

Mu'tazilite Disputes on Pure Motive and Knowledge of Agency
Razi presents the second and third Mu'tazilite internal disputes on pure motive and knowledge type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite disagreements on whether the act becomes necessary with a pure motive, and whether our knowledge of our own agency is necessary or theoretical.

Core Idea
Even among Mu'tazilites, there is disagreement on necessity and the source of self-knowledge.

After establishing that some Mu'tazilites make the act dependent on a motive, a further question arises: does the act become necessary when the pure motive (dāʿiya khāliṣa) is present? Additionally, the Mu'tazilites debated whether our knowledge that we create our own actions is innate or requires reasoning. This passage explores those two issues.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the terms داعية خالصة, واجب الوقوع, أولى بالوقوع, ضروري, نظري.
2
Understand the second dispute
This sub-dispute asks: when the pure motive is present, does the act become necessary? Abu al-Husayn would say yes, Mahmud al-Khwarazmi says it only becomes more deserving.
3
Analyze the third dispute
The third point is about epistemology: is our knowledge that we create our own acts necessary (self-evident) or theoretical (requiring reasoning)? Most Mu'tazilites say theoretical.
4
Compare the two positions
Note how the Mu'tazilites differ among themselves on these details, showing that their school is not monolithic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The majority of Mu'tazilites say knowledge that you are the creator of your own acts is theoretical, not self-evident. When did you last truly examine whether you are the agent of your actions? If this knowledge requires reasoning, have you done that reasoning? If not, what would it mean to live as if you haven't yet proven your own responsibility?
?
Consider the phrase 'more deserving of occurrence' (أولى بالوقوع). In a moment of weakness, you might excuse sin by saying 'the motive was too strong.' But does that excuse you, or does it only make your sin more deserving? Are you willing to let God judge you by the same standard—that your sin was 'more deserving' but not necessary—and still hold you accountable?

The Coincidence Argument: Identical Conditions Lead to Determinism
Refuting the Mu'tazilī claim that action follows power and motivation without determinism (first part).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the initial argument shows that if an act occurs or not under identical conditions, it is pure coincidence and thus determinism.

Core Idea
If conditions are the same, the act's occurrence is not in the servant's control.

The Mu'tazilī claims that human action arises from power and motivation without determinism. The author begins by testing this claim: suppose power and motivation are continuously present. The act can either occur or not. Since nothing changes, the occurrence is pure chance, and the servant has no control — that is determinism.

Guided Path
1
Read the scenario setup
Understand that the argument assumes power+motivation are present continuously and the act is possible.
2
Note the two possibilities
The act sometimes occurs and sometimes does not under identical conditions.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Since no new factor arises, the act is pure coincidence, and the servant has no power to bring it about. This is determinism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقدير. عند حصول مجموع القدرة مع الداعي، يمكن أن يحصل الفعل تارة، وأن لا يحصل أخرى. لأن كل ما كان ممكناً، لم يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال. فليفرض أن القدرة مع الداعي كان حاصلا، واستمرا. ثم تارة حصل الفعل، وأخرى لم يحصل. فهذا الحصول ما كان من العبد البتة، بل كان المحض الاتفاق من غير سبب. لأن مجموع القدرة والداعي لما حصل بتمامه. عند عدم الفعل تارة. وعند حصوله أخرى، ولم يتجدد أمر من الأمور عند حدوث الفعل، حتى يقال: الفعل إنما حدث في ذلك الوقت لأجله. فعلى هذا التقدير يكون حدوث الفعل في ذلك الوقت محض الاتفاق، ولم يكن ذلك في وسعه، ولا في اختياره. وهذا أيضاً: محض الجبر. لأنه لا سبيل له البتة إلى إيقاع الفعل، بل إن اتفق وقوعه مع ذلك القدر من القدرة والداعي، فقد وقع. وإن لم يتفق وقوعه لم يقع. ولم يكن البتة رجحان أحد الطرفين على الآخر، بأمر من جهة العبد البتة. فثبت: أن المعتزلي. إن قال: إن وقوع الفعل عقيب القدرة والداعي: واجب البتة فقد قال بالجبر، من حيث لا يشعر به وإن قال: إنه غير واجب. فقد قال أيضاً بالجبر، من حيث لا يشعر به فيثبت: أنه لا يمكنه أن يعبر عن نفي الجبر بعبارة معلومة، إلا وتلك العبارة مشتملة على الجبر فإن قال قائل: إني أقول: إن عند حصول القدرة والداعي يقع الفعل، ولا أقول: إن ذلك الوقوع واجب أو غير واجب. وهذا القدر يكفيني في التعبير عن مذهبي. فنقول: هب أنك لا تذكر إلا الوقوع. إلا أنا نقول: ذلك الوقوع. إما أن يكون مع الوجوب، أو لا مع الوجوب. وعلى كل من التقديرين: فالجبر لازم. فكان الجبر لازماً على كل التقديرات. فهذا بحث شريف لا بد من الوقوف عليه في هذا الباب.
Consideration: When the combination of power (qudra) and motivation (dā'ī) is present, it is possible that the act sometimes occurs and sometimes does not. Because everything that is possible does not necessitate absurdity from assuming its occurrence. So let it be assumed that power with motivation was present and continued. Then sometimes the act occurred and sometimes it did not. This occurrence is not from the servant at all, but rather it is pure coincidence without cause. Because when the combination of power and motivation is fully present, at the time of non-existence of the act sometimes, and at its occurrence other times, and no new thing occurs at the time of the act's occurrence such that it could be said: the act only occurred at that time for its sake. On this assumption, the occurrence of the act at that time would be pure coincidence, and it would not be within his capacity nor his choice. And this too is pure determinism (jabr). Because he has no way at all to bring about the act; rather, if its occurrence happens to coincide with that amount of power and motivation, it occurs; and if its occurrence does not coincide, it does not occur. And there is no preponderance of one side over the other by anything from the servant at all. So it is established that the Mu'tazilī: if he says that the occurrence of the act after power and motivation is absolutely necessary, then he has affirmed determinism without realizing it; and if he says that it is not necessary, then he has also affirmed determinism without realizing it. So it is established that he cannot express the negation of determinism with any known expression except that this expression includes determinism. For if someone says: I say that when power and motivation are present the act occurs, and I do not say that this occurrence is necessary or not necessary, and this amount suffices me in expressing my doctrine. We say: Grant that you only mention occurrence. Yet we say: that occurrence is either with necessity or without necessity. On both assumptions, determinism is necessary. So determinism was necessary on all assumptions. This is a noble investigation that must be understood in this chapter.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents a demonstration (burhān) that the Mu'tazilī position on human action inevitably entails determinism (jabr), whether they claim the act is necessary or not. The argument begins by considering the scenario where power (qudra) and motivation (dā'ī) are both present and continuous. Since the act is possible, we can hypothetically suppose that sometimes the act occurs and sometimes it does not. But since the conditions are identical in both cases and no new factor intervenes, the occurrence of the act becomes pure coincidence (muḥḍ al-ittifāq), not within the servant's capacity or choice. This is pure determinism because the servant has no control over whether the act occurs or not. The argument then refutes the Mu'tazilī claim that the act is neither necessary nor non-necessary; the response forces the Mu'tazilī to admit that the occurrence is either with necessity or without it, and both lead to determinism. The passage concludes that the Mu'tazilī cannot articulate a coherent denial of determinism without implicitly affirming it.

Key Terms
قدرة
داع
جبر
اتفاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilī thinks that if the act occurs after power and motivation, it is within the servant's control even if conditions are identical.
Correction: The argument shows that with identical conditions, occurrence is coincidence, not choice.

Checkpoint
In the hypothetical scenario, what is the state of the act when power and motivation are present continuously?
A) The act always occurs.
B) The act never occurs.
C) The act sometimes occurs and sometimes does not.
D) The act occurs only if a new factor appears.

Key Takeaway
Under identical conditions, the occurrence of the act is pure coincidence, not within the servant's control—this is determinism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have both the ability and desire to do something but choose not to, do you truly believe that choice is yours? How does this argument challenge that belief, and what does it mean for your responsibility before Allah?
?
If the occurrence of your actions under identical conditions is pure coincidence, then what distinguishes obedience from sin? How can you truly repent or strive if the outcome is not within your control?
?
Consider a sin you committed where you had both power and motivation. According to this passage, was that sin really your choice, or was it just an uncaused event? How does that realization affect your understanding of divine justice?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
Both Claims of Necessity and Non-Necessity Lead to Determinism
Demonstrating that both necessity and non-necessity claims lead to determinism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how claiming the act is necessary after power+motivation, or claiming it is not necessary, both result in determinism.

Core Idea
Whether the Mu'tazilī calls the act necessary or not, determinism follows.

Having shown that assuming the act can occur or not under identical conditions leads to determinism, the author now addresses the Mu'tazilī's possible responses. If the Mu'tazilī claims the act is absolutely necessary after power and motivation, that is determinism. If he claims it is not necessary, that too is determinism, because the act's occurrence would still be uncontrolled. Either way, the Mu'tazilī cannot avoid determinism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two options
Notice that the author presents two possibilities: the Mu'tazilī say the act is necessary (wājib) or not necessary.
2
Examine the first option
If he says necessary, then the act must occur when power+motivation are present, which is determinism.
3
Examine the second option
If he says not necessary, then the act is possible but its occurrence is still not controlled, also determinism.
4
Conclude the dilemma
Either way, the Mu'tazilī cannot articulate a denial of determinism without affirming it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقدير. عند حصول مجموع القدرة مع الداعي، يمكن أن يحصل الفعل تارة، وأن لا يحصل أخرى. لأن كل ما كان ممكناً، لم يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال. فليفرض أن القدرة مع الداعي كان حاصلا، واستمرا. ثم تارة حصل الفعل، وأخرى لم يحصل. فهذا الحصول ما كان من العبد البتة، بل كان المحض الاتفاق من غير سبب. لأن مجموع القدرة والداعي لما حصل بتمامه. عند عدم الفعل تارة. وعند حصوله أخرى، ولم يتجدد أمر من الأمور عند حدوث الفعل، حتى يقال: الفعل إنما حدث في ذلك الوقت لأجله. فعلى هذا التقدير يكون حدوث الفعل في ذلك الوقت محض الاتفاق، ولم يكن ذلك في وسعه، ولا في اختياره. وهذا أيضاً: محض الجبر. لأنه لا سبيل له البتة إلى إيقاع الفعل، بل إن اتفق وقوعه مع ذلك القدر من القدرة والداعي، فقد وقع. وإن لم يتفق وقوعه لم يقع. ولم يكن البتة رجحان أحد الطرفين على الآخر، بأمر من جهة العبد البتة. فثبت: أن المعتزلي. إن قال: إن وقوع الفعل عقيب القدرة والداعي: واجب البتة فقد قال بالجبر، من حيث لا يشعر به وإن قال: إنه غير واجب. فقد قال أيضاً بالجبر، من حيث لا يشعر به فيثبت: أنه لا يمكنه أن يعبر عن نفي الجبر بعبارة معلومة، إلا وتلك العبارة مشتملة على الجبر فإن قال قائل: إني أقول: إن عند حصول القدرة والداعي يقع الفعل، ولا أقول: إن ذلك الوقوع واجب أو غير واجب. وهذا القدر يكفيني في التعبير عن مذهبي. فنقول: هب أنك لا تذكر إلا الوقوع. إلا أنا نقول: ذلك الوقوع. إما أن يكون مع الوجوب، أو لا مع الوجوب. وعلى كل من التقديرين: فالجبر لازم. فكان الجبر لازماً على كل التقديرات. فهذا بحث شريف لا بد من الوقوف عليه في هذا الباب.
Consideration: When the combination of power (qudra) and motivation (dā'ī) is present, it is possible that the act sometimes occurs and sometimes does not. Because everything that is possible does not necessitate absurdity from assuming its occurrence. So let it be assumed that power with motivation was present and continued. Then sometimes the act occurred and sometimes it did not. This occurrence is not from the servant at all, but rather it is pure coincidence without cause. Because when the combination of power and motivation is fully present, at the time of non-existence of the act sometimes, and at its occurrence other times, and no new thing occurs at the time of the act's occurrence such that it could be said: the act only occurred at that time for its sake. On this assumption, the occurrence of the act at that time would be pure coincidence, and it would not be within his capacity nor his choice. And this too is pure determinism (jabr). Because he has no way at all to bring about the act; rather, if its occurrence happens to coincide with that amount of power and motivation, it occurs; and if its occurrence does not coincide, it does not occur. And there is no preponderance of one side over the other by anything from the servant at all. So it is established that the Mu'tazilī: if he says that the occurrence of the act after power and motivation is absolutely necessary, then he has affirmed determinism without realizing it; and if he says that it is not necessary, then he has also affirmed determinism without realizing it. So it is established that he cannot express the negation of determinism with any known expression except that this expression includes determinism. For if someone says: I say that when power and motivation are present the act occurs, and I do not say that this occurrence is necessary or not necessary, and this amount suffices me in expressing my doctrine. We say: Grant that you only mention occurrence. Yet we say: that occurrence is either with necessity or without necessity. On both assumptions, determinism is necessary. So determinism was necessary on all assumptions. This is a noble investigation that must be understood in this chapter.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents a demonstration (burhān) that the Mu'tazilī position on human action inevitably entails determinism (jabr), whether they claim the act is necessary or not. The argument begins by considering the scenario where power (qudra) and motivation (dā'ī) are both present and continuous. Since the act is possible, we can hypothetically suppose that sometimes the act occurs and sometimes it does not. But since the conditions are identical in both cases and no new factor intervenes, the occurrence of the act becomes pure coincidence (muḥḍ al-ittifāq), not within the servant's capacity or choice. This is pure determinism because the servant has no control over whether the act occurs or not. The argument then refutes the Mu'tazilī claim that the act is neither necessary nor non-necessary; the response forces the Mu'tazilī to admit that the occurrence is either with necessity or without it, and both lead to determinism. The passage concludes that the Mu'tazilī cannot articulate a coherent denial of determinism without implicitly affirming it.

Key Terms
قدرة
داع
جبر
وجوب
غير واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the passage conclude about any expression the Mu'tazilī uses to deny determinism?
A) Some expressions can avoid determinism.
B) All expressions used to deny determinism end up affirming it.
C) Only the expression 'necessary' leads to determinism.
D) Only the expression 'not necessary' leads to determinism.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilī cannot avoid determinism by claiming either necessity or non-necessity of the act after power and motivation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often assume your choices are entirely your own. But if even the Mu'tazilī's framework leads to determinism, where does that leave your sense of control? Are you willing to admit that your most 'free' decisions are actually dependent on Allah's will?
?
The Mu'tazilī claims to defend human freedom, yet his own words trap him. What if your own understanding of free will is similarly self-defeating? Will you examine your beliefs honestly, or cling to comfortable illusions that absolve you of submission?
?
If every expression about action contains determinism, then you cannot truly describe yourself as an independent agent. How does that change the way you approach your daily choices—your prayers, your sins, your reliance on Allah? What would it mean to live as if every breath is a gift, not a right?

The Final Rebuttal: Even Stating 'Occurrence' Without Modality Leads to Determinism
Responding to the objection that the act simply occurs without modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the reply to the objection that 'the act simply occurs' still entails determinism.

Core Idea
If the act occurs, it must be either with necessity or without, both lead to determinism.

A Mu'tazilī might think they can avoid the dilemma by simply saying 'the act occurs when power and motivation are present' without calling it necessary or not necessary. The author counters by asking: Is that occurrence with necessity or without? In either case, determinism follows. Thus, no escape is possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector says: I only say the act occurs, without further qualification.
2
Understand the reply
The author forces the objector to admit that the occurrence is either with necessity or without.
3
Apply the previous dilemma
If with necessity, it's deterministic; if without, it's still deterministic as shown earlier.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
Determinism is necessary on all assumptions.

The Mu'tazili Objection on Divine Determinism
Mu'tazili objection that jabr implies necessary agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili argument that the Ash'ari doctrine of jabr logically entails God being a necessary agent, and the resulting dilemma between two coherent positions.

Core Idea
Consistency demands either determinism in both realms or in neither.

The Mu'tazila launch a powerful counter-attack: if determinism (jabr) is true for human actions based on power and motivation, then the same logic applied to God would make Him a necessary agent (mūjib bi-dh-dhāt), contradicting the Ash'ari insistence on divine choice. This forces an uncomfortable choice between two extremes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili claim
Begin by reading the Mu'tazili assertion that determinism only works if God is an agent by essence. Then read the full passage and translation.
Notice the key terms: جبر, موجب بالذات, فاعل بالاختيار.
2
Trace the logical analogy
The Mu'tazili applies the determinist's own reasoning about human action (power + motivation → necessary act) to God's action. Follow this analogy step by step.
The argument leads to the pre-eternity of the world if the preconditions are eternal.
3
Identify the two coherent options
The Mu'tazili presents only two consistent views: determinism in both the witnessed and unseen (philosophers) or negation in both (Mu'tazila). Recognize why the Ash'ari mixed position is labeled contradictory.
The Ash'aris affirm jabr for humans but deny it for God.
4
Consider the author's rebuttal
The author notes that even if this objection is strong, the Mu'tazili position (al-qadar) faces an even uglier problem: requiring preponderance without a preponderant.
This sets up the next discussion on the necessity of a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني: لقائل من المعتزلة أن يقول: الجبر لا يتأتى إلا مع القول بكون الباري موجباً بالذات لا فاعلا بالاختيار. لأن حاصل كلام الجبرية هو: أن عند حصول القدرة والداعية الخالصة يجب الفعل، وعند فقدان هذا المجموع يمتنع الفعل. وهذا هو القول بالجبر. إلا أن هذا الكلام بعينه وارد في فاعلية الله. لأن كل ما لا بد منه في فاعلية الله تعالى. إن كان حاصلا في الأزل، وجب حصول العالم معه، وإن لم يكن أزلياً، افتقر حدوثه إلى سبب آخر، ولا يتسلسل بل ينتهي إلى الصفات الأزلية. وحينئذ يعود هذا السؤال. قالوا: فثبت: أن دليلكم في إثبات الجبر، إن تم وكمل فهو، يوجب القول بالجبر شاهداً أو غائباً. وذلك يوجب القول يقدم العالم، ويكونه تعالى موجباً بالذات، لا فاعلا بالاختيار. كما هو قول الفلاسفة. وأما إن قلنا: إن صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على الداعية، وإن توقف عليها لكن صدور الفعل عند حصول الداعية غير واجب. فهذا قول المعتزلة. وبطل القول بالجبر في الشاهد والغائب فثبت: أن الحق. إما القول بالجبر شاهداً وغائباً، كما هو قول الفلاسفة. أو القول بنفيه شاهداً وغائباً، كما هو قول المعتزلة. فأما القول بإثباته في الشاهد، ونفيه في الغائب، كما هو قولكم فكلام متناقض. فهذا هو الكلام الأقوى من جانبهم. واعلم: أن بتقدير أن يتوجه على القول بالجبر هذا الإلزام، إلا أن القول بالقدر يتوجه عليه، ما هو أقبح منه وأفحش. وذلك لأنا بينا: أن القول بالقدر لا يتم إلا إذا قيل: إن رجحان الفاعلية على التاركية، يحصل لا لمرجح. وعند هذا القول. إما أن يكون الإمكان محوجاً إلى مؤثر، أو لا.
The second investigation: One of the Mu'tazila may say: Determinism (jabr) only occurs with the assertion that the Creator is an agent by essence (mūjib bi-dh-dhāt), not an agent by choice (fā'il bi-l-ikhtiyār). Because the gist of the determinists' speech is that when power (qudra) and pure motivation (dā'īyya khāliṣa) are present, the act must occur, and when this combination is absent, the act is impossible. And this is the statement of determinism. But this same speech applies to God's agency. Because if everything necessary for God's agency was present from eternity, the existence of the world would be necessary with it; and if it was not eternal, its origination would need another cause, and there is no infinite regress but it ends with eternal attributes. And then this question returns. They said: So it is established that your proof for affirming determinism, if it is complete and perfect, necessitates affirming determinism in the witnessed (shāhid) and the unseen (ghā'ib). And that necessitates affirming the pre-eternity of the world and that God is an agent by essence, not an agent by choice, as is the philosophers' doctrine. And if we say that the occurrence of the act from the capable (al-qādir) does not depend on motivation, or if it depends on it but the occurrence of the act at the presence of motivation is not necessary, then this is the Mu'tazila's doctrine, and the statement of determinism is invalidated in the witnessed and the unseen. So it is established that the truth is either the statement of determinism in the witnessed and the unseen, as is the philosophers' doctrine, or the statement of its negation in the witnessed and the unseen, as is the Mu'tazila's doctrine. As for the statement of affirming it in the witnessed and negating it in the unseen, as is your statement, it is contradictory speech. This is the strongest argument from their side. And know that even if this objection is directed against the statement of determinism, the statement of power (al-qadar) is faced with something uglier and more repugnant than it. That is because we have shown that the statement of power is only complete if it is said that the preponderance of effecting over refraining occurs without a preponderant (lā li-murajjiḥ). And under this statement, either possibility needs an efficient cause or not.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage presents the second investigation (al-baḥth ath-thānī), which is a Mu'tazilī objection against the Ash'arī position on determinism. The Mu'tazilī argues that determinism (jabr) necessarily follows only if one holds that God is an agent by essence (mūjib bi-dh-dhāt) rather than an agent by choice (fā'il bi-l-ikhtiyār). The argument is that the determinists' own reasoning about human action—that when power and motivation are present the act necessarily occurs—applies equally to God's action. If the preconditions of God's action are eternal, then the world's existence is necessary; if not, there is an infinite regress ending in eternal attributes, leading again to necessity. Thus the determinist's proof leads to the philosophers' view of the world's pre-eternity and God as necessary agent. The Mu'tazilī then presents two coherent options: either determinism in both the witnessed (human) and unseen (divine) realms (the philosophers), or negation of determinism in both (the Mu'tazila). The Ash'arī mixed position—determinism in the human realm but not in the divine—is contradictory. The author then notes that even if this objection is strong against determinism, the position of power (al-qadar, i.e., Mu'tazilī free will) faces an even more severe problem: it requires that the preponderance of acting over refraining occur without any preponderant (lā li-murajjiḥ), which is absurd because it would mean something possible can be actualized without a cause. The passage ends mid-sentence discussing whether possibility itself needs an efficient cause.

Key Terms
جبر
موجب بالذات
فاعل بالاختيار
شاهد
غائب
قدرة
داعية
ترجيح
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume the determinist argument only applies to human actions and doesn't affect divine agency.
Correction: The Mu'tazili objection shows that the same reasoning about power and motivation, if applied consistently to God, leads to the conclusion that God is a necessary agent and the world is pre-eternal—a view the Ash'aris reject.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilī objection, what follows if the determinist's argument is applied consistently to both human and divine action?
A) God becomes an agent by choice.
B) The world must be pre-eternal and God is an agent by essence.
C) Determinism is negated in the unseen.
D) The Ash'arī position is vindicated.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'arī position of affirming determinism in humans but not in God is logically inconsistent; consistency demands either determinism in both the witnessed and unseen or its negation in both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you willing to accept that your human experience of choice may be an illusion, as the determinists argue, because you cannot prove your action is not necessitated? How does this challenge your personal accountability before Allah — and will you investigate this tonight until you have some clarity?
?
The passage shows that either both realms are deterministic or both are free. Which consistent position do you find more compelling, and does your intellectual honesty force you to accept its implications for God's agency and the eternity of the world? What then is the state of your belief right now — are you ready to revise it?
?
If you find the Mu'tazili objection compelling, are you prepared to reject your own sense of moral responsibility and divine justice as they understand it? Or if you reject it, can you articulate a non-contradictory response without relying on comfortable contradictions? What will you do with the tension this passage creates in your heart?

The Dilemma of Qadar: Free Will Leads to Worse Consequences
Sanusi presents a dilemma against the Mu'tazilite qadar argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that the Mu'tazilite position on free will (qadar) is logically inconsistent, leading either to determinism (jabr) or atheism.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite doctrine of qadar collapses into either jabr or atheism — there is no logical middle ground.

This passage presents a powerful critique of the Mu'tazilite concept of free will. Even if one concedes that determinism (jabr) has problems, the Ash'ari author argues that the qadar position is far worse. By analyzing the logical consequences of the principle of 'imkān' (possibility), he shows that the Mu'tazila cannot consistently maintain that some actions are self-determined while others require a cause. This dilemma forces the rational thinker to choose between a consistently deterministic universe or a universe with no Creator at all.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's use of conditional statements to build the dilemma.
2
Identify the two branches of the dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities: either possibility (imkān) requires an effect or it does not. Trace each branch and its consequence (jabr or atheism).
3
Understand the Mu'tazilite inconsistency
The Mu'tazila try to take a middle ground, but the author shows that their position is contradictory because it applies the principle inconsistently.
This is the key refutation.
4
Reflect on the outcome
Ask yourself: If the Mu'tazilite qadar is inconsistent, what does that imply for the free will debate? The author promises a future clarification on the seen vs. unseen.

واعلم : أن بتقدير أن يتوجه على القول بالجبر هذا الإلزام ، إلا أن القول بالقدر يتوجه عليه ، ما هو أقبح [ منه (۲) ] وأفحش . وذلك لأنا بينا : أن القول بالقدر لا يتم إلا إذا قيل : إن رجحان الفاعلية على التاركية ، يحصل لا لمرجح . وعند هذا القول . إما أن يكون [ الإمكان محوجاً إلى مؤثر ، أو لا يكون (۳) ] كذلك. فإن كان الإمكان محوجاً إلى المؤثر ، لزم احتياج كل ممكن إلى المرجح . وعلى هذا ، يكون رجحان الفاعلية على التاركية ، لا بد وأن يكون معللاً بعلة موجبة . وحينئذ يلزم الجبر . وأما إن كان الإمكان غير محوج إلى المرجح ، فعند ذلك لا خفاء أن يستدل بالإمكان على المؤثر . وحينئذ [ يكرم (٢) ] نفي الصانع بالكلية . سواء كان موجباً أو مختاراً . فثبت أن [ القول (۳) ] الحق : إما القول باحتياج جميع الممكنات إلى المؤثر ، وحينئذ يلزم الجبر . أو القول باستغناء جميع الممكنات عن المؤثر ، وحينئذ يلزم نفي المؤثرات أصلا . فأما القول بأن الإمكان محوج إلى المؤثر في موضع دون موضع ، كما هو قول المعتزلة : فهو قول متناقض باطل . فثبت : أنه إن لزم على القول بالجبر : إثبات أن مؤثر العالم موجب بالذات ، لا فاعل بالاختيار ، لزم (4) على القول بالقدر أن يكون رجحاناً وجود العالم على عدمه لا لمؤثر أصلاً - والأول - وإن كان قبيحاً ـ فلا شك أن هذا الأخير أقبح منه ، وأفحش بكثير على أنا سنذكر الفرق اليقيني بين الشاهد والغائب إن شاء الله تعالى .
And know that: even if it is assumed that this objection applies to the statement of jabr, yet the statement of qadar (free will) is subject to something uglier and more obscene. That is because we have shown that the statement of qadar is only complete if it is said that the preponderance of agency (fā‘iliyya) over abstention (tārkiyya) occurs without a determinant (murjih). And upon this statement, either possibility (imkān) necessitates an effect (mu’aththir), or it does not. If possibility necessitates the effect, then every possible thing must need a determinant, and on this basis, the preponderance of agency over abstention must be caused by a necessitating cause, and then jabr follows. But if possibility does not necessitate a determinant, then it is not hidden that one argues from possibility to the effect, and then the denial of the Maker (ṣāni‘) altogether follows, whether He be a necessitating agent or a voluntary agent. So it is established that the true statement is either: the statement that all possibles need an effect, in which case jabr follows; or the statement that all possibles are independent of the effect, in which case the negation of effects altogether follows. As for the statement that possibility necessitates an effect in some cases but not in others, as is the statement of the Mu‘tazila, it is a contradictory and false statement. So it is established: if it follows from the statement of jabr the affirmation that the effecter of the world is an agent by essence, not a voluntary agent, then it follows from the statement of qadar that the preponderance of the world's existence over its non-existence is without any effecter at all. The first, though ugly, undoubtedly this latter is uglier and much more obscene. Moreover, we will mention the decisive difference between the seen and the unseen, God willing.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage presents a second, stronger argument against the Mu'tazila position of qadar. The author, an Ash'ari, argues that even if the previous objection against jabr were valid, the qadar position leads to worse consequences. He restates the core of qadar: the preponderance of action over inaction occurs without a determinant. Then he poses a dilemma regarding the principle of 'imkān' (possibility) requiring an effect. If possibility necessitates an effect (mu’aththir), then every possible thing requires a determinant, leading to jabr. If possibility does not necessitate a determinant, then the argument from possibility to the existence of a Creator collapses, leading to atheism. Therefore, the only consistent positions are: either all possibles need an effect (jabr) or none need it (atheism). The Mu'tazila inconsistently apply the principle only in some cases. Thus, the qadar position leads either to jabr or to atheism, both worse than the philosopher's position (which at least affirms a necessary agent). The author promises a future clarification on the difference between seen and unseen.

Key Terms
قدر (qadar)
إمكان (imkān)
مؤثر (mu’aththir)
مرجح (murjih)
راجحية الفاعلية على التاركية (preponderance of agency over abstention)
نفي الصانع (nafy al-ṣāni‘)
الشاهد والغائب (al-shāhid wa al-ghā’ib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite qadar position is a consistent middle ground between jabr (determinism) and atheism.
Correction: This argument shows that qadar is logically inconsistent because it applies the principle of possibility only selectively, leading either to jabr or to atheism.

Checkpoint
In this argument, what does the author claim is the consequence if possibility (imkān) does NOT necessitate an effect?
A) It leads to jabr
B) It leads to the denial of the Creator altogether
C) It leads to the Mu'tazila position
D) It leads to the philosophers' position

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite qadar position is logically untenable because it inconsistently applies the principle of possibility to some events but not others, leading to contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the logical extremes presented in this lesson—complete determinism (jabr) and atheism—which do you find more personally troubling, and why? Does your own understanding of God's will and human choice lean toward one extreme, or do you find yourself in a contradictory middle ground like the Mu'tazilites? What is one inconsistency in your own beliefs that this argument exposes?
?
The Mu'tazilites insisted that human actions require no determinant from God, effectively making man the sole author of his deeds. Have there been moments when you felt completely self-sufficient in your decisions, forgetting that even your ability to choose is a gift from God? How would it change your daily reliance on Him if you truly believed that every choice is dependent on His will?
?
If someone accused Islam of fatalism (jabr), which horn of this dilemma would you use to defend God's justice and power? But more importantly, does your life reflect a humble submission to God's decree, or do you secretly crave the autonomy that the Mu'tazilites championed? Name one area where you need to surrender more fully to God's will.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
8 min
The Necessity of a Determinant for Human Action
Author presents a proof that the servant's action depends on a determinant from God, concluding the servant is not independent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that the servant's action depends on a determinant from God, and why the servant is not independent.

Core Idea
Human action must have a determinant; that determinant cannot come from the servant alone.

In this passage, the author employs dialectical reasoning to establish a foundational principle of kalam: every action requires a preponderating factor. By eliminating impossible scenarios, he demonstrates that the determinant must originate from God, thereby affirming divine causality without negating human acquisition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
The author starts with the fact that power (qudra) is equally capable of action and inaction, creating a need for a determinant to explain why one occurs.
2
Follow the divisions
He divides the issue into two possibilities: preponderance depends on a determinant or not. He eliminates the second possibility because it leads to denial of the Maker and compulsion.
3
Examine the determinant's source
Then he examines the determinant itself: it must be from the servant, another, or neither. He shows it cannot be from the servant (leads to regress or compulsion) nor from neither (leads to uncaused occurrence).
4
Conclude the proof
Thus the determinant must come from another (God). The author then explains that when God creates the determinant, the action becomes necessary, establishing the servant's non-independence.
This aligns with the Ash'ari concept of kasb (acquisition).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
