Necessity of Inclination and Conception
Author proves necessity of third and fourth ranks and concludes four ranks necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why inclination and conception are necessary conditions for voluntary action, based on self-evident knowledge and logical necessity.

Core Idea
Pleasure is sought and pain is avoided by nature; conception makes benefit known.

The author continues to establish the psychological prerequisites for voluntary action. Having shown that belief in benefit/harm and decisive resolve are necessary, he now demonstrates that inclination toward benefit and aversion from harm are known by self-evident necessity, and that conception of the thing is an inevitable precondition for any such judgment. This lesson shows how these two final ranks complete the set of four necessary premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Notice it treats the third rank first, then the fourth, and ends with a conclusion.
2
Identify the self-evident knowledge for the third rank
The author asserts that inclination toward benefit and aversion from harm are known necessarily. He gives the example that pleasure and joy are desired in themselves, and pain and grief are fled from in themselves. Note that this is a claim about human nature, not learned behavior.
Think about whether you agree that this is self-evident.
3
Understand the logical argument for the fourth rank
The author argues that without conception (taṣawwur) of a thing, it is impossible to know it as beneficial or harmful. Since knowledge of benefit/harm is needed for desire or flight, conception is a necessary prerequisite.
This is a logical chain: conception → perception of benefit/harm → desire/flight.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that these premises (the four ranks) are necessary. Think about how the four ranks form a complete set of conditions for voluntary action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المرتبة الثالثة : وهي أنه لابد من حصول الميل إلى ما يعتقد فيه كونه نفعا ، وحصول النفرة عما يعتقد فيه كونه ضررا . فهذا أمر معلوم بالضرورة . فإنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن اللذة والسرور مطلوبان بالذات ، والألم والغم مهروب عنهما بالذات . وأما المرتبة الرابعة : وهي التصور. وهذا أيضا أمر لا بد منه . فإنه إذا لم يصر ذلك الشيء متصورا ، امتنع العلم بكونه نافعا ، وكونه ضارا . وما لم يحصل هذا الإدراك ، لم يحصل الطلب والهرب . فثبت : أن هذه المقدمات لازمة
As for the third rank: it is that there must be an inclination towards what is believed to be beneficial, and an aversion from what is believed to be harmful. This is a matter known necessarily, for we know necessarily that pleasure and joy are desired in themselves, and pain and grief are fled from in themselves. As for the fourth rank: it is conception. This also is inevitable. For if that thing is not conceived, knowledge of it being beneficial and of it being harmful is impossible. And as long as this perception does not occur, desire and flight do not occur. So it is established that these premises are necessary.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage provides proof for the necessity of the third and fourth ranks using self-evident (ḍarūrī) knowledge. For the third rank (inclination and aversion), the author states it is self-evident that pleasure and joy are sought intrinsically, and pain and grief are avoided intrinsically—this is a basic fact of human nature. For the fourth rank (conception), he argues that without conceiving (taṣawwur) of a thing, one cannot know it as beneficial or harmful; without that perception, desire (ṭalab) and flight (harab) cannot occur. Therefore, conception is a necessary prerequisite. The passage concludes that these premises (the four ranks) are necessary (lāzima) for voluntary action. This completes the demonstration of the psychological conditions.

The Eighth Proof: Against Infinite Regress and Determinism
Argument against infinite regress leading to determinism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof against infinite regress forces the conclusion that human actions must be necessarily determined by God's creation of attributes.

Core Idea
If human power could cause actions, infinite regress would force a first action determined by God.

This passage presents a sophisticated proof (the eighth in a series) that argues for determinism (al-jabr). The author begins by considering what would happen if human power could truly produce an effect. He shows that the only way to avoid an infinite chain of causes is to posit a first action that is not caused by another human action. Then he examines the conditions of that first action, leading to the conclusion that its occurrence is necessary given the combination of the servant's essence and God-given attributes. This argument is central to the theological debate on human free will and divine predestination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice the structure: it starts with a conditional, then argues against regress, then analyzes sufficiency.
The passage is incomplete; it ends while considering the alternative.
2
Identify the logical moves
Trace the argument: If x then y leads to regress → contradiction; therefore must have a first action; then analyze whether servant's essence+attributes is sufficient; if sufficient, effect is necessary → determinism.
This is a typical kalam proof structure.
3
Reflect on the concept of jabr
The proof concludes that the effect is necessary, meaning determinism. Consider how this relates to human responsibility and the role of God's creation.
This connects to broader theological issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثامن: لو كان يحدث بقدرة العبد شيء، لامتنع أن يقال إنه إنما صدر عنه هذا الفعل لأنه صدر عنه فعل آخر. وإلا لزم التسلسل وهو محال. بل لا بد وأن تنتهي أفعال العبد إلى فعل أول صدر عنه ذلك الفعل، لا بواسطة فعل آخر منه. فنقول: صدور ذلك الفعل عنه لا بد وأن يكون لأجل اتصاف ذاته بصفات حصلت بتخليق الله. فنقول: ذاته مع مجموع تلك الأمور المعتبرة في كونه موجداً لذلك، إما أن يكون كافياً في صدور ذلك الأثر عنه أو لا يكون. فإن كان كافياً، كان الفعل مع واجباً. إذ لو لم يجب، لجاز أن يتخلف. ولو جاز أن يتخلف، لكان صدور الأثر عن ذلك المجموع ممكناً لا واجباً. فكان يفتقر إلى زائد آخر. ولو كان كذلك، لما كان الحاصل قبل ذلك كافياً في صدور الأثر عنه. وكنا قد فرضناه كذلك. هذا خلف. فثبت أن حصول الأثر عند ذلك المجموع يكون واجباً. وهو الجبر. وأيضاً: إذا كان حصول الأثر عند حصول ذلك المجموع غير واجب...
The eighth proof: If something were to occur by the power of the servant, it would be impossible to say that this action originated from him only because another action originated from him; otherwise infinite regress (التسلسل) would follow, which is impossible. Rather, the actions of the servant must end at a first action that originated from him without mediation of another action from him. So we say: The origination of that action from him must be due to his essence being qualified with attributes that came about by the creation of God. So we say: His essence, along with the totality of those considered matters in his being a composer of that, either is sufficient for the origination of that effect from him or not. If it is sufficient, then the action is necessary (واجباً). For if it were not necessary, it would be possible to lag; and if it were possible to lag, then the origination of the effect from that totality would be possible, not necessary. Then it would need an additional thing. If that were so, then what was obtained before would not be sufficient for the origination of the effect from him. But we had assumed it to be so. This is a contradiction (خلف). So it is established that the obtaining of the effect at that totality is necessary, and that is al-jabr (الجبر). And also: If the obtaining of the effect at the obtaining of that totality is not necessary...
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof (البرهان الثامن) in a series, arguing for determinism (الجبر). It begins by considering a hypothetical: if something were to occur by the servant's power, one could not claim that the action occurs solely because a previous action occurred, as that would lead to infinite regress (تسلسل). To avoid regress, there must be a first action that is not caused by another human action. This first action must originate from the servant's essence qualified by attributes created by God. The author then analyzes whether the combination of the servant's essence and these God-given attributes is sufficient to produce the effect. If sufficient, the effect must necessarily occur (otherwise it would lag and require an additional cause, contradicting sufficiency). Therefore, the effect is necessary, and this is determinism. The passage ends by considering the alternative (if the combination is not sufficient), but the text is incomplete.

الجبر
قدرة العبد
خلق
صفات
الفعل الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof completely denies human choice and responsibility.
Correction: The proof does not deny human choice but shows that any action ultimately traces back to God's creation of attributes.

Checkpoint
Why must there be a first action not caused by a previous human action according to the proof?
A) Because actions cannot be caused by human power
B) To avoid infinite regress (التسلسل)
C) Because God created all actions directly
D) Because the servant's power is insufficient

Key Takeaway
This proof argues that to avoid infinite regress, the first human action must arise necessarily from the servant's God-given attributes, establishing determinism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that even the first action you perform is dependent on God's creation of your attributes. Do you live as if your abilities are self-made, or do you constantly acknowledge that every skill and moment of strength is a gift from God? What would change if you truly internalized that?
?
The proof argues that if your attributes were sufficient to produce an action, that action would be necessary, leaving no room for delay. Have you ever felt a sense of urgency or necessity in your actions that made you realize you are not as in control as you think? How does that feeling align with the idea of divine determination?
?
If your actions are ultimately necessary due to God's creation of your attributes, then every sin you commit is also part of this chain. Does this thought make you feel absolved of responsibility, or does it drive you to seek God's mercy more earnestly? How does this lesson change the way you repent?

The ninth proof: refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that humans can create miracles
Refutes Mu'tazilite argument that only God creates bodies and life, showing its weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that only God can create bodies and miracles, and why the author considers it flawed.

Core Idea
Only God can create bodies—but the Mu'tazilite proof for this is itself fragile.

This passage presents the author's refutation of a key Mu'tazilite claim: that humans cannot create miracles because they cannot create bodies. The Mu'tazilites offer a proof based on the nature of power, dividing it into power by essence and power by acquisition. The author exposes the weakness of this reasoning, challenging its premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the flow
Read the Arabic passage once for overall structure. Then read the translation. Note the flow: challenge to Mu'tazilites, their response with a proof (two parts), then the author's dismissal.
The passage ends mid-sentence with the beginning of a counter-argument.
2
Examine the Mu'tazilite proof
Focus on the two arguments: against powerful by essence (because bodies are similar) and against powerful by power (two reasons showing power cannot create bodies). Understand each sub-argument.
The proof relies on the principle that the judgment of a thing applies to its like.
3
Assess the author's critique
Note how the author dismisses the entire Mu'tazilite argument as extremely weak and begins to question its basis. Reflect on why he considers it insufficient.
The author's counter starts with 'Why is it not permissible that it be...' suggesting a flaw in the universality of the premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان التاسع نقول للمعتزلة : إذا جوزتم كون العبد موجداً فما الأمان من أن يكون محدث المعجزات واحداً من الشياطين والأبالسة وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يخرج المعجز من أن يدل على الصدق قالوا الدليل دل على أن غير الله لا يقدر على خلق الجسم والحياة وتقرير ذلك الدليل أن نقول لو قدر أحد من الخلق على ذلك لكان ذلك القادر إما أن يكون قادراً لذاته وإما أن يكون قادراً بالقدرة والقسمان باطلان فبطل القول بكون أحد من الخلق قادراً على خلق الجسم وعلى خلق الحياة وإنما قلنا إنه لا يجوز أن يكون قادراً لذاته لأن ذلك القادر لا بد وأن يكون جسماً والأجسام متماثلة فلو كان الجسم قادراً لذاته لكان كل كذلك ضرورة أن حكم الشيء حكم مثله ولما بطل هذا علمنا أنه لا يجوز أن يكون قادراً لذاته وإنما قلنا إنه لا يجوز أن يكون قادراً بالقدرة لأن القدرة لا تصلح بفعل الجسم ويدل عليه وجهان الأول إن القدرة التي لنا لا تصلح لفعل الأجسام فهذا الامتناع حكم مشترك فيه بين القدرة التي لنا والحكم المشترك لا بد من تعليله بوصف مشترك فيه وما ذاك إلا كونها قدراً فثبت أن كون القدرة قدرة مانعة من صلاحية إيجاد الجسم فوجب أن تكون كل قدرة كذلك الثاني إن كل قدرة نفرضها فهي إما أن تكون مثلاً لهذه القدرة الحاصلة لنا أو مخالفاً لها فإن كانت مثلاً لهذه القدرة وجب أن لا تكون صالحة للإيجاد كما أن هذه القدرة غير صالحة للإيجاد ضرورة أن حكم الشيء حكم مثله وإن كانت مخالفة لهذه القدر لم تكن مخالفتها لهذه القدرة أشد من مخالفة بعض هذه القدر لبعض فلما كانت هذه القدر مع كون بعضها مخالفاً للبعض لا يصلح للإيجاد فكذلك تلك القدرة المفروضة وجب أن لا تصلح للإيجاد هذا حاصل ما لخصه القوم وعولوا عليه في أن فاعل المعجزات هو الله تعالى وهو كلام في غاية الرخاوة والسقوط فنقول لم لا يجوز أن يكون
The Ninth Proof: We say to the Mu'tazilites: If you permit that the servant is an originator (mujid), then what assurance is there that the producer of miracles (mujizat) might be one of the devils and satans? And on this estimation, the miracle would cease to indicate truthfulness. They said: The evidence indicates that no one other than God is able to create the body and life. The exposition of that evidence is that we say: If any of the creation had power over that, then that powerful one would either be powerful by essence or powerful by power (qudra). Both divisions are false, so the statement that any of the creation is able to create the body and create life is false. We said that it is not permissible for him to be powerful by essence, because that powerful one must be a body, and bodies are similar. So if a body were powerful by essence, then every body would be likewise, necessarily because the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like. When this was proven false, we knew that it is not permissible for him to be powerful by essence. And we said that it is not permissible for him to be powerful by power, because power is not suitable for the act of a body. Two aspects indicate this: First: The power that we possess is not suitable for the act of bodies. So this impossibility is a common judgment among the power that we possess. A common judgment must be attributed to a common description, and that is nothing other than its being power (qadr). So it is established that the fact that power is power prevents it from being suitable for bringing a body into existence. Therefore every power must be likewise. Second: Every power we suppose is either similar to this power that we possess or different from it. If it is similar to this power, then it must not be suitable for bringing into existence, just as this power is not suitable for bringing into existence, necessarily because the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like. And if it is different from this power, its difference from this power is not more severe than the difference of some of these powers from others. Since these powers, despite some of them differing from others, are not suitable for bringing into existence, then likewise that supposed power must not be suitable for bringing into existence. This is the gist of what they summarized and relied upon, that the doer of miracles is God Almighty. And it is speech of the utmost weakness and collapse. So we say: Why is it not permissible that it be
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents the Ninth Proof, a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position. The argument begins with a challenge: if you allow the servant to be an originator, what prevents devils from producing miracles, thereby undermining the evidential value of miracles? The Mu'tazilites respond with a proof that only God can create bodies and life. They argue that any creature capable of such creation would either be powerful by essence or by power. The first is impossible because the creature must be a body, and all bodies are similar; if one body were powerful by essence, all would be, which is false. The second is impossible because power (qudra) is not suitable for the act of a body. They give two reasons: (1) Our power does not affect bodies, and this impossibility is shared by all powers due to the common attribute of being power, so all powers lack this capacity. (2) Any supposed power is either like ours (so equally incapable) or different; but differences among powers (like among human powers) do not alter their incapacity to create bodies, so the supposed power would also be incapable. The author then dismisses this reasoning as extremely weak and fragile, and begins to counter by asking why it is not permissible that the power be specific. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the author's repudiation is clear.

Key Terms
قادر بالذات
قادر بالقدرة
قدرة
جسم
معجزات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite argument is that humans cannot create miracles because they lack all power.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites argue specifically that power cannot create bodies, not that humans lack all power. The author challenges the universality of the premise that power is inherently incapable of creating bodies.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite argument presented, why cannot a creature be powerful by essence to create a body?
A) Because only God can be powerful by essence.
B) Because all bodies are similar, so if one body were powerful by essence, all bodies would be, which is false.
C) Because power by essence requires being immaterial.
D) Because the creature would need a power that is not a body.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite proof that only God creates bodies relies on the impossibility of a creature being powerful by essence or by power, but the author dismisses this reasoning as extremely weak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on an argument for your faith that, upon honest reflection, feels 'extremely weak' (في غاية الرخاوة)? What is one belief you hold about God that you have not fully examined? Will you take time today to examine its foundations?
?
If a group of scholars can produce a proof that the author himself calls 'collapsed', how much more careful should you be with the arguments you accept or present? What is one argument you frequently use that you have not checked for logical flaws?
?
The Mu'tazilites feared that if a satan could produce miracles, trust in divine signs would collapse. Do you have a fear that if one of your religious certainties were shaken, your faith might fall apart? What does this tell you about the strength of your faith's foundation?

The Agent of Miracles Need Not Be a Body: Non-Material Existents
The author denies the necessity of bodily agency and presents non-material existents as counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that the agent of a miracle does not have to be a body, appealing to philosophers' proofs of non-material existents.

Core Idea
The agent of a miracle could be a rational soul, not necessarily a body.

In this passage, the author begins by challenging the assumption that the agent of a miracle must be a body. He cites the philosophers who affirm non-material intellects and souls, and suggests the rational soul as a plausible agent. The burden of proof is on those who insist on a bodily agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the dialectical style: the author presents an objection and then refutes it.
2
Identify the main claim being denied
The author denies that the agent must be a body. Find where he states this.
3
Note the appeal to philosophers
The philosophers affirm non-material existents. List them from the passage.
These are intellects, celestial souls, and rational souls.
4
Consider the rational soul alternative
The author suggests the rational soul as a possible agent. Why does he think this is plausible?
Because souls differ in essence, power is not transferable.
5
Identify the burden of proof
The opponent must prove that everything besides God is either located or inhering. Why is this important?
Without that proof, non-material agency remains possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك الفاعل قادراً لذاته لكان كل جسم كذلك. ضرورة أن الأجسام متماثلة، لكنا لا نسلم أن ذلك الفاعل يجب أن يكون جسماً. وذلك لأن الفلاسفة يثبتون موجودات ليست متحيزة ولا حالة في المتحيز، كالعقول والنفوس الفلكية، والنفوس الناطقة. فما لم تقيموا الدلالة على فساد هذا التقسيم، لا ندرك الجزم بأن ذلك الفاعل يجب أن يكون جسماً. والمتكلمون ما ذكروا في إبطال هذا القسم كلاماً يصلح أن يلتفت إليه. بل نقول: لم لا يجوز أن تكون النفس الناطقة التي لهذا الشيء، هي التي أحدثت هذا المعجز. وما كانت نفسه مخالفة لسائر النفوس في الماهية، لم يلزم من قدرته على تلك الأفعال قدرة غيره عليها. وبالجملة: فهذا الاحتمال لا يندفع إلا بإقامة الدلالة على أن كل ما سوى الله تعالى، فهو إما متحيز أو حال فيه. سلمنا: أن ذلك الفاعل يجب أن يكون جسماً. فلم قلتم: إن الأجسام كلها متماثلة؟ وما الدلالة على ذلك؟ وتقريره: إن المعلوم هو أن الأجسام متساوية في وجوب الحصول في الحيز، وفي كونها بحيث يمنع غيرها عن أن يحصل بحيث هو، وفي كونها قابلة للأعراض. إلا أن ذلك إشارة إلى الأحكام واللوازم. وقد ثبت: أن الأشياء المختلفة في الماهيات، لا يمتنع اشتراكها في لازم واحد. فلم قلتم: إن جميع الأجسام بأسرها متساوية في ماهياتها حتى يتم لكم ما ذكرتم؟ سلمنا: أن الأجسام بأسرها متساوية في تمام الماهية. لكن لم قلتم: إن الأفراد المتساوية في تمام الماهية، يجب أن يصح على كل واحد منها ما يصح على الآخر؟ بيانه: وهو أن تلك الأفراد، وإن كانت متساوية في الماهية، لكنها مختلفة في التشخص والتعين، وإلا لكان كل واحد عين الآخر. وحينئذ لا يبقى التعدد. وإذا كان كذلك، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك التعين شرطاً لحصول ذلك الحكم، في أحد الطرفين، أو مانعاً منه في الطرف الآخر؟ سلمنا: أن التعين ساقط الاعتبار، لكن قولكم: ويصح على الشيء ما يصح على مثله، منقوض بصور كثيرة: الأول: إن أفراد النوع الأخير متساوية في تمام الماهية. مع أن تعين هذا يمنع حصوله لذاك، وتعين ذاك يمتنع حصوله لهذا. وإلا لصار هذا عين ذاك، وذاك عين هذا. وكل ذلك محال. فمحال أن يكون مقدور العبد مثل مقدور الله تعالى. ثم لم يلزم منه أن يكون مقدور العبد بحيث أن يكون مقدوراً لله تعالى - على مذهب المعتزلة - لأن عندهم مقدور واحد بين قادرين محال. الثالث: إن مشايخ المعتزلة: مذهبهم: أن الذوات متساوية في كونها ذوات. وإنما يخالف بعضها بعضاً لأجل اختصاص كل واحد منها بصفته الخاصة. فعلى هذا: ذات الله تعالى مساوية لسائر الذوات في الذاتية. ثم لم يلزم منه أن يصح على كل واحد من الذوات كل ما يصح على سائر الذوات. وإلا لزم أن يصح على ذات الله: الحدوث والإمكان، والحاجة إلى المحل، وأن يصح على ذوات المحدثات كونها قديمة، واجبة الوجود. وكل ذلك محال. الرابع: إن صفة الوجود - عندهم - صفة واحدة. فيكون الوجود الحاصل في السواد والبياض: مساوياً للوجود الحاصل في ذات الله. ولم يلزم الاستواء في جميع الأحكام.
That agent would be powerful in itself, so every body would be likewise, necessarily because bodies are similar. But we do not concede that that agent must be a body. This is because the philosophers affirm existents that are neither located in space nor inhering in what is located, such as intellects, celestial souls, and rational souls. As long as you have not established evidence for the invalidity of this division, we do not attain certainty that that agent must be a body. And the theologians (mutakallimūn) have not mentioned in refuting this division any speech worthy of attention. Rather we say: Why is it not permissible that the rational soul belonging to this thing is what produced this miracle? And since its soul differs from other souls in essence, it does not follow from its power over those acts that another has power over them. In short: this possibility is not repelled except by establishing evidence that everything other than God is either located in space or inhering in what is located. We concede: that agent must be a body. Then why do you say that all bodies are similar? And what is the evidence for that? Its elaboration: What is known is that bodies are equal in the necessity of occupying space, in being such that they prevent others from occupying their position, and in being receptive to accidents. However, that refers to properties and concomitants. And it has been established that things differing in essences are not prevented from sharing a single concomitant. So why do you say that all bodies entirely are equal in their essences, so that what you mentioned is complete for you? We concede: that bodies are entirely equal in complete essence. But why do you say that individuals equal in complete essence must have whatever is valid for one be valid for the other? Its explanation: that these individuals, even if equal in essence, differ in individuation and specification; otherwise each would be identical to the other, and then multiplicity would not remain. If that is so, why is it not permissible that that specification is a condition for obtaining that ruling in one of the two sides, or an obstacle to it in the other side? We concede: that specification is disregarded. But your statement: 'what is valid for a thing is valid for its like' is refuted by many cases: First: individuals of the lowest species are equal in complete essence, even though the specification of one prevents its occurrence in another, and the specification of the other prevents its occurrence in this one; otherwise this would become that, and that would become this, and all that is impossible. So it is impossible that the power of the servant be like the power of God. Then it does not follow from it that the power of the servant is such that it is within God's power—according to the doctrine of the Mu'tazila—because in their view a single power between two agents is impossible. Third: The masters of the Mu'tazila hold that essences are equal in being essences; they differ only by each being distinguished by its own attribute. On this basis, the essence of God is equal to other essences in essentiality. Yet it does not follow from this that whatever is valid for each essence is valid for all other essences; otherwise it would follow that contingency, possibility, and need for a substrate are valid for God's essence, and that the essences of contingent things have eternity and necessary existence. All that is impossible. Fourth: The attribute of existence—in their view—is a single attribute. So the existence that inheres in blackness and whiteness is equal to the existence inhering in the essence of God. Yet equality in all rulings does not follow.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation of the claim that the agent of a miracle must be a body (jism). The author, likely Ibn al-Maṭālib or similar, adopts a dialectical style, presenting and answering objections. The passage opens with an objection: if the agent were powerful in itself, then every body would be similarly powerful since bodies are alike; but the author immediately counters by denying the premise that the agent must be a body. He appeals to the philosophers' proof of non-material existents like intellects and souls. The theologians' refutation of this division is inadequate. He then poses a positive alternative: why not the rational soul? Since souls differ in essence, power over an act does not imply another's equal power. The only way to rule out this possibility is to prove that everything besides God is either located or inhering. The author then concedes the agent must be a body, but challenges the claim that all bodies are alike, noting that shared properties (like occupying space) are merely concomitants and do not prove sameness of essence. Even if essences are equal, individuation (ta'ayyun) can affect rulings. Next, he concedes individuation as irrelevant, but attacks the principle 'what is valid for one is valid for its like' with multiple counterexamples: (1) individuals of the same species are equal in essence but their individuation prevents interchangeability, so the power of a servant cannot equal that of God; (2) the Mu'tazilite view that essences are equal leads to absurdities like contingency applying to God; (3) the unity of the attribute of existence does not imply equality of all rulings. The passage thus systematically undermines the necessity of a bodily agent through logical and metaphysical arguments.

جسم
متماثلة
الأجسام
النفس الناطقة
المتكلمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The agent of any action must be a body.
Correction: The author points to philosophers' non-material existents and the rational soul as counterexamples, showing that non-corporeal agency is possible.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it not necessary that the agent of a miracle be a body?
A) Because the philosophers have proved that non-material existents like souls exist.
B) Because all bodies are alike in essence.
C) Because the theologians have decisively refuted the possibility of non-material agents.
D) Because the miracle itself proves the agent is a body.

Key Takeaway
The author argues that the agent of a miracle need not be a body because non-material agents like rational souls are possible, and the burden of proof lies on those who insist on corporeality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that a rational soul, not a body, could be the agent of a miracle. Does your daily life treat your soul as capable of actions beyond physical limits, or do you reduce your agency to your body? What would change if you truly believed your soul could act independently of your body?
?
The author says the burden of proof is on those who insist on corporeality. Where in your life do you insist on material explanations when a non-material cause is possible? Will you now consider spiritual causality more seriously?
?
The passage mentions that souls differ in essence, so power over one miracle does not imply equality. Are you content to let God's miracles remain unique, or do you demand that all believers have the same powers? What does that say about your trust in God's wisdom?

Are All Bodies Alike? Challenging the Similarity of Bodies
The author challenges the claim that all bodies are similar in essence, distinguishing between properties and true nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author challenges the claim that all bodies are essentially alike, distinguishing between shared properties and essence.

Core Idea
Shared properties like occupying space do not prove sameness of essence.

The author now concedes that the agent must be a body, but immediately questions whether all bodies are essentially similar. He points out that bodies share certain concomitants (e.g., occupying space), but these do not entail identity of essence. The opponent must prove that bodies are equal in their true essences.

Guided Path
1
Read the concession and the new question
The author concedes the agent must be a body but asks: 'Why do you say all bodies are similar?'.
Note the shift in argument.
2
Identify the distinction between property and essence
The author notes that bodies share properties like occupying space, but these are mere concomitants (lazim), not the essence.
3
Understand the logical move
Things with different essences can share a single concomitant, so shared properties don't prove sameness of essence.
This undermines the opponent's position.
4
Follow the concessions
The author concedes bodies are equal in essence, but then raises individuation (ta'ayyun) as a factor that can affect rulings.
5
Recognize the final concession for argument's sake
He then concedes individuation is disregarded, moving to the next refutation.
This shows the dialectical strategy.

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation of the claim that the agent of a miracle must be a body (jism). The author, likely Ibn al-Maṭālib or similar, adopts a dialectical style, presenting and answering objections. The passage opens with an objection: if the agent were powerful in itself, then every body would be similarly powerful since bodies are alike; but the author immediately counters by denying the premise that the agent must be a body. He then poses a positive alternative: why not the rational soul? Since souls differ in essence, power over an act does not imply another's equal power. The author then concedes the agent must be a body, but challenges the claim that all bodies are alike, noting that shared properties (like occupying space) are merely concomitants and do not prove sameness of essence. What does the author use to show that shared properties do not prove sameness of essence?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a person you admire for their external practices—prayer, charity, fasting. Have you ever assumed that because they share these visible actions with you, they must share your internal state of imān? How does this passage's warning about shared properties versus essence challenge you to avoid that assumption, and what will you do this week to look beyond surfaces?
?
The author shows that even if two things have the same essence, individuation (their specific identity) can make a difference. Reflect on your own relationship with Allah: do you ever treat His attributes (like mercy or power) as if they are just like human attributes, only bigger? How would your worship change if you truly believed that His essence is utterly unique and that no similarity in properties implies similarity in nature? What specific step can you take today to deepen your reverence for His transcendence?
?
Consider a moment when you felt that your own repeated sins made you identical to someone else who commits them. Did you despair of Allah's mercy, thinking that your essence was 'bad'? This passage explains that even in essences, individuation matters. How can you use this truth to resist the lie that your past defines your identity, and instead turn to Allah with renewed hope, knowing that He sees you as a unique individual? What concrete change in your thoughts or actions will you make right now?

Counterexamples to the Principle of Similarity
The author provides four counterexamples to disprove the principle of similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four counterexamples the author presents to refute the principle that what is valid for one thing is valid for its like.

Core Idea
The principle of similarity fails because of individuation, divine transcendence, and the nature of existence.

The author now attacks the core principle that underlies the opponent's argument: 'what is valid for a thing is valid for its like.' He provides multiple counterexamples from theology and metaphysics, showing the principle leads to absurdities.

Guided Path
1
Read the counterexamples carefully
The author presents three main counterexamples. Read each one in Arabic and translation.
The text numbers them first, third, fourth.
2
Understand the first counterexample
Individuals of the same species differ by individuation, so power of servant cannot equal God's power.
3
Understand the second counterexample
Using Mu'tazilite doctrine: essences are equal in essentiality, but attributes differ; otherwise God would have contingency.
This shows the principle leads to blasphemous conclusions.
4
Understand the third counterexample
Existence is one attribute, but it does not imply equality of all rulings; proof is that black and white share existence but differ.
5
Synthesize the overall refutation
The principle is false in multiple domains, thus the argument for bodily agency collapses.

ذلك الفاعل قادراً لذاته لكان كل جسم كذلك. ضرورة أن الأجسام متماثلة، لكنا لا نسلم أن ذلك الفاعل يجب أن يكون جسماً. وذلك لأن الفلاسفة يثبتون موجودات ليست متحيزة ولا حالة في المتحيز، كالعقول والنفوس الفلكية، والنفوس الناطقة. فما لم تقيموا الدلالة على فساد هذا التقسيم، لا ندرك الجزم بأن ذلك الفاعل يجب أن يكون جسماً. والمتكلمون ما ذكروا في إبطال هذا القسم كلاماً يصلح أن يلتفت إليه. بل نقول: لم لا يجوز أن تكون النفس الناطقة التي لهذا الشيء، هي التي أحدثت هذا المعجز. وما كانت نفسه مخالفة لسائر النفوس في الماهية، لم يلزم من قدرته على تلك الأفعال قدرة غيره عليها. وبالجملة: فهذا الاحتمال لا يندفع إلا بإقامة الدلالة على أن كل ما سوى الله تعالى، فهو إما متحيز أو حال فيه. سلمنا: أن ذلك الفاعل يجب أن يكون جسماً. فلم قلتم: إن الأجسام كلها متماثلة؟ وما الدلالة على ذلك؟ وتقريره: إن المعلوم هو أن الأجسام متساوية في وجوب الحصول في الحيز، وفي كونها بحيث يمنع غيرها عن أن يحصل بحيث هو، وفي كونها قابلة للأعراض. إلا أن ذلك إشارة إلى الأحكام واللوازم. وقد ثبت: أن الأشياء المختلفة في الماهيات، لا يمتنع اشتراكها في لازم واحد. فلم قلتم: إن جميع الأجسام بأسرها متساوية في ماهياتها حتى يتم لكم ما ذكرتم؟ سلمنا: أن الأجسام بأسرها متساوية في تمام الماهية. لكن لم قلتم: إن الأفراد المتساوية في تمام الماهية، يجب أن يصح على كل واحد منها ما يصح على الآخر؟ بيانه: وهو أن تلك الأفراد، وإن كانت متساوية في الماهية، لكنها مختلفة في التشخص والتعين، وإلا لكان كل واحد عين الآخر. وحينئذ لا يبقى التعدد. وإذا كان كذلك، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك التعين شرطاً لحصول ذلك الحكم، في أحد الطرفين، أو مانعاً منه في الطرف الآخر؟ سلمنا: أن التعين ساقط الاعتبار، لكن قولكم: ويصح على الشيء ما يصح على مثله، منقوض بصور كثيرة: الأول: إن أفراد النوع الأخير متساوية في تمام الماهية. مع أن تعين هذا يمنع حصوله لذاك، وتعين ذاك يمتنع حصوله لهذا. وإلا لصار هذا عين ذاك، وذاك عين هذا. وكل ذلك محال. فمحال أن يكون مقدور العبد مثل مقدور الله تعالى. ثم لم يلزم منه أن يكون مقدور العبد بحيث أن يكون مقدوراً لله تعالى - على مذهب المعتزلة - لأن عندهم مقدور واحد بين قادرين محال. الثالث: إن مشايخ المعتزلة: مذهبهم: أن الذوات متساوية في كونها ذوات. وإنما يخالف بعضها بعضاً لأجل اختصاص كل واحد منها بصفته الخاصة. فعلى هذا: ذات الله تعالى مساوية لسائر الذوات في الذاتية. ثم لم يلزم منه أن يصح على كل واحد من الذوات كل ما يصح على سائر الذوات. وإلا لزم أن يصح على ذات الله: الحدوث والإمكان، والحاجة إلى المحل، وأن يصح على ذوات المحدثات كونها قديمة، واجبة الوجود. وكل ذلك محال. الرابع: إن صفة الوجود - عندهم - صفة واحدة. فيكون الوجود الحاصل في السواد والبياض: مساوياً للوجود الحاصل في ذات الله. ولم يلزم الاستواء في جميع الأحكام.
That agent would be powerful in itself, so every body would be likewise, necessarily because bodies are similar. But we do not concede that that agent must be a body. This is because the philosophers affirm existents that are neither located in space nor inhering in what is located, such as intellects, celestial souls, and rational souls. As long as you have not established evidence for the invalidity of this division, we do not attain certainty that that agent must be a body. And the theologians (mutakallimūn) have not mentioned in refuting this division any speech worthy of attention. Rather we say: Why is it not permissible that the rational soul belonging to this thing is what produced this miracle? And since its soul differs from other souls in essence, it does not follow from its power over those acts that another has power over them. In short: this possibility is not repelled except by establishing evidence that everything other than God is either located in space or inhering in what is located. We concede: that agent must be a body. Then why do you say that all bodies are similar? And what is the evidence for that? Its elaboration: What is known is that bodies are equal in the necessity of occupying space, in being such that they prevent others from occupying their position, and in being receptive to accidents. However, that refers to properties and concomitants. And it has been established that things differing in essences are not prevented from sharing a single concomitant. So why do you say that all bodies entirely are equal in their essences, so that what you mentioned is complete for you? We concede: that bodies are entirely equal in complete essence. But why do you say that individuals equal in complete essence must have whatever is valid for one be valid for the other? Its explanation: that these individuals, even if equal in essence, differ in individuation and specification; otherwise each would be identical to the other, and then multiplicity would not remain. If that is so, why is it not permissible that that specification is a condition for obtaining that ruling in one of the two sides, or an obstacle to it in the other side? We concede: that specification is disregarded. But your statement: 'what is valid for a thing is valid for its like' is refuted by many cases: First: individuals of the lowest species are equal in complete essence, even though the specification of one prevents its occurrence in another, and the specification of the other prevents its occurrence in this one; otherwise this would become that, and that would become this, and all that is impossible. So it is impossible that the power of the servant be like the power of God. Then it does not follow from it that the power of the servant is such that it is within God's power—according to the doctrine of the Mu'tazila—because in their view a single power between two agents is impossible. Third: The masters of the Mu'tazila hold that essences are equal in being essences; they differ only by each being distinguished by its own attribute. On this basis, the essence of God is equal to other essences in essentiality. Yet it does not follow from this that whatever is valid for each essence is valid for all other essences; otherwise it would follow that contingency, possibility, and need for a substrate are valid for God's essence, and that the essences of contingent things have eternity and necessary existence. All that is impossible. Fourth: The attribute of existence—in their view—is a single attribute. So the existence that inheres in blackness and whiteness is equal to the existence inhering in the essence of God. Yet equality in all rulings does not follow.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation of the claim that the agent of a miracle must be a body (jism). The author, likely Ibn al-Maṭālib or similar, adopts a dialectical style, presenting and answering objections. The passage opens with an objection: if the agent were powerful in itself, then every body would be similarly powerful since bodies are alike; but the author immediately counters by denying the premise that the agent must be a body. He appeals to the philosophers' proof of non-material existents like intellects and souls. The theologians' refutation of this division is inadequate. He then poses a positive alternative: why not the rational soul? Since souls differ in essence, power over an act does not imply another's equal power. The only way to rule out this possibility is to prove that everything besides God is either located or inhering. The author then concedes the agent must be a body, but challenges the claim that all bodies are alike, noting that shared properties (like occupying space) are merely concomitants and do not prove sameness of essence. Even if essences are equal, individuation (ta'ayyun) can affect rulings. Next, he concedes individuation as irrelevant, but attacks the principle 'what is valid for one is valid for its like' with multiple counterexamples: (1) individuals of the same species are equal in essence but their individuation prevents interchangeability, so the power of a servant cannot equal that of God; (2) the Mu'tazilite view that essences are equal leads to absurdities like contingency applying to God; (3) the unity of the attribute of existence does not imply equality of all rulings. The passage thus systematically undermines the necessity of a bodily agent through logical and metaphysical arguments.

Key Terms
التعين
الماهية
المعتزلة
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things are alike in essence, whatever is true of one is true of the other.
Correction: The author shows via multiple examples that individuation, attributes, and existence preclude this principle.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the fourth counterexample show?
A) Existence is one attribute but does not imply equality of rulings.
B) Essences are different.
C) Bodies are different.
D) The agent must be a body.

Key Takeaway
The principle 'what is valid for one is valid for its like' is refuted by multiple counterexamples from individuation, divine attributes, and existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's power is incomparable, or do you sometimes measure His abilities by human standards? Take one area of your life where you doubt God's power and explicitly affirm His transcendence today.
?
The Mu'tazila argued that God's essence is like others in essentiality—but then denied the logical consequences. Have you ever held a principle that logically leads to a conclusion you reject? How honest are you with yourself about the implications of your beliefs?
?
If existence is one attribute yet its manifestations differ, do you treat God's presence in your life as common and ordinary? Commit to a specific act of reverence that acknowledges His uniqueness.

Response to an Objection: Similarity of Powers Does Not Imply Same Ruling
Sanusi refutes an objection about power similarity with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two ways to refute the claim that if powers are similar, they must have the same ruling regarding ability to create a body.

Core Idea
Difference in kind breaks the analogy; causation lies in specific quiddities, not general similarity.

The opponent argues that if an extraordinary power differs from ordinary powers, its difference cannot be more severe than the differences among ordinary powers themselves. Since ordinary powers cannot create bodies, this extraordinary power also cannot. Sanusi responds with two powerful rebuttals, showing that the analogy fails on multiple levels.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the opponent's claim and Sanusi's two-part refutation.
The passage is a direct rebuttal, so follow the argument flow carefully.
2
Understand the first response
The first response shows that the extraordinary power's difference is one of kind (fit vs. unfit), not just degree. This makes the analogy invalid.
The author points out that claiming otherwise is the very point in dispute.
3
Understand the second response
Even if the difference were not more severe, the inability to create a body is due to a specific quiddity present in ordinary powers, not their mere difference.
Thus the opponent's generalization fails because the cause is specific, not universal.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you grasp the key difference between the two responses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ثانياً: « تلك القدرة إذا كانت مخالفة لهذه القدر، لم تكن مخالفتها لهذه القدر التي عندنا أشد من مخالفة بعض هذه القدر لبعض. وإذا كانت هذه القدر، مع مخالفة بعضها البعض، لا تصلح لخلق الجسم، فكذلك تلك القدرة وجب أن لا تصلح لخلق الجسم». فنقول: هذا أيضاً في غاية السقوط. وبيانه من وجوه: الأول: لا نسلم أن مخالفة تلك القدرة المفروضة، لهذه القدر، ليست مخالفة بعضها البعض. فإن من الجائز أن يقال: إن هذه القدر - وإن كان بعضها يخالف بعضها - إلا أنها متشاركة في أنها لا تصلح لخلق الجسم، أما تلك القدرة المفروضة فهي مخالفة الجملة هذه القدر. من حيث إن تلك القدرة صالحة لخلق الجسم، وجملة هذه القدر، لا تصلح لخلق الجسم. وكانت مخالفة هذه القدرة الجملة هذه القدر، أشد من مخالفة بعض هذه القدر لبعض. فإن ادعيتم أن الأمر ليس كذلك، فهو عين محل النزاع. الثاني: سلمنا أن مخالفة تلك القدرة لهذه القدر، ليست أشد من مخالفة بعض هذه القدر لبعض. إلا أن هذه القدر، لها ماهيات مخصوصة، ولها أيضاً: أن بعضها مخالف لبعض. فعلى هذا: الامتناع ليس هو كون بعضها مخالفاً للبعض، حتى يطرد هذا الحكم في جميع القدر، التي يخالف بعضها بعضاً. بل علة هذا الامتناع: تلك الماهية المخصوصة. ولما كانت تلك الماهية المخصوصية، غير حاصلة في سائر القدر: سقط ما ذكرتم.
And as for his second saying: 'If that power were different from these powers, its difference from these powers which we have would not be more severe than the difference of some of these powers from others. And if these powers, despite the difference of some from others, are not fit for the creation of a body, then likewise that power must not be fit for the creation of a body.' We say: This too is extremely weak. Its explanation is from two aspects: First: We do not accept that the difference of that supposed power from these powers is not a difference of some from others. For it is permissible to say that these powers—even if some differ from others—share in not being fit for body creation, while that supposed power differs from the whole of these powers, in that that power is fit for body creation and the whole of these powers are not fit for body creation. Thus the difference of that power from the whole of these powers is more severe than the difference of some of these powers from others. If you claim the matter is not so, that is precisely the point of dispute. Second: We concede that the difference of that power from these powers is not more severe than the difference of some of these powers from others. However, these powers have specific quiddities, and also some of them differ from others. So upon this, the impossibility is not due to some being different from others, such that this ruling would apply to all powers that differ from each other. Rather, the cause of this impossibility is that specific quiddity. And since that specific quiddity is not present in other powers, what you mentioned falls away.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage refutes another objection against the possibility of an acquired power that can create a body. The opponent argues that since all ordinary powers differ from each other but nonetheless cannot create bodies, any power that differs from them must also lack that ability. The author responds with two points. First, the difference between the supposed power (which can create) and ordinary powers is not merely like the difference among ordinary powers; it is a difference in kind (fit vs. unfit), which is more fundamental. Calling it merely one of degree is the very point in dispute. Second, even if we grant that the difference is not more severe, the inability to create is not due to mere difference but to a specific quiddity present in ordinary powers and absent in the supposed power. Thus the opponent's analogy fails.

Key Terms
قدرة
مخالفة
خلق الجسم
ماهيات مخصوصة
علة الامتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the opponent's analogy is logical, but the author shows it conflates generic difference with specific causation.
Correction: The opponent assumes that all differences among powers are equivalent, but the author demonstrates that a difference in kind (fit vs. unfit) or a difference in the cause (specific quiddity) invalidates the generalization.

Checkpoint
The author's first response to the opponent's second saying is that the difference of the supposed power is...
A) identical to the difference among ordinary powers
B) more severe because it is a difference between fit and unfit
C) less severe because it is only a minor variation
D) irrelevant to the argument

Key Takeaway
The opponent's analogy fails because the extraordinary power differs in kind and the cause of inability is a specific quiddity, not mere difference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your life have you assumed that because something looks similar on the surface, it must have the same outcome—and what did that cost you?
?
The author says the real cause is a hidden quiddity, not the obvious difference. What hidden assumption about your own abilities might be holding you back from a spiritual breakthrough?
?
If you truly believed that God's power to create is utterly unique and not comparable to your power, how would you approach your own limitations differently today?

The Fourth Mu'tazilite Argument on Power
The author refutes the fourth Mu'tazilite argument on power's nature as a preventative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite fourth argument concerning power as a preventative and the author's refutation based on the problematic premise of 'the ruling of a thing applies to its like.'

Core Idea
A flawed universal analogy undermines the Mu'tazilite argument about power.

This lesson examines the fourth Mu'tazilite argument that all powers must prevent certain actions because power inherently has that quality. The author refutes it by exposing the weakness in the premise that the ruling of a thing applies to its like. We'll see why this logical leap fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation. Get a feel for the argument's structure and the author's concise style.
Notice the use of 'ورابعها' indicating this is the fourth argument in a series.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
The argument states that since power (qudra) as such has the ruling of being a preventative (manī'a), then every power must be a preventative of that ruling. Pinpoint this deduction.
3
Recognize the underlying principle
The argument relies on the principle 'ḥukm al-shay' ḥukm mithlih' (the ruling of a thing applies to its like). Understand why this is a general logical premise.
This principle is often used in kalām but has limitations.
4
Understand the author's refutation
The author simply states that they have already exposed the problems with this premise. Consider why this suffices as a refutation within the broader discussion.
The refutation is concise because the premise has been previously critiqued.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها : إن كون القدرة قدرة ، لما كانت على هذا الحكم ، وجب أن تكون كل قدرة مانعة من هذا الحكم. وهذا بناء على أن حكم الشيء حكم مثله . وقد بينا ما في هذه المقدمة
And the fourth of them: that power, in that it is power, since it is upon this ruling, it is necessary that every power is a preventative of this ruling. And this is based on the principle that the ruling of a thing is the ruling of its like. And we have already shown what is [problematic] in this premise.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is part of a refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that only God can create bodies. The author states the fourth argument of the opponent: since power (qudra) as power has the property of being a preventative (manī'a) of a particular action (in this context, the creation of a body), then every power must prevent that action. This is based on the general logical principle that the ruling of a thing applies to its like (ḥukm al-shay' ḥukm mithlih). The author then simply notes that they have already exposed the weakness of this premise, thus refuting the argument.

Key Terms
القدرة
المانعة
حكم الشيء حكم مثله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a property of one specific power (preventative) can be universalized to all powers without further justification.
Correction: The principle that the ruling of a thing applies to its like is not universally valid and has been shown to be problematic in this context.

Checkpoint
What principle does the fourth Mu'tazilite argument rely upon?
A) That power is a substance.
B) That the ruling of a thing applies to its like.
C) That all powers are identical.
D) That power cannot create bodies.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite argument fails because it uncritically applies the principle that the ruling of a thing applies to its like, which the author has already shown to be problematic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a mistake in reasoning, do you trace it back to the principles you assumed? The Mu'tazilites assumed a principle without checking. What is one principle you assume in your daily life that, if wrong, could lead you to serious error? Will you examine it today?
?
The author quickly dismissed the argument by pointing out a flawed premise. How often do you accept arguments from authority or tradition without scrutinizing their foundations? What would it take for you to have the courage to question a deeply held belief, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
This lesson shows a logical flaw in a sophisticated theological argument. In your own life, where might you be using flawed logic to justify your actions or beliefs? Can you identify a situation where you have said 'this worked before, so it must work now' without considering differences? Commit to one area where you will stop assuming sameness and start examining.

The First Argument: Difference in Capacity
First argument: denial of equal difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that a differing power cannot create bodies.

Core Idea
Difference in capacity can be greater than mere difference in kind.

The Mu'tazilites argued that if powers differ among themselves yet none can create bodies, then any differing power also cannot. In response, the author denies that this new power's difference is merely like the others. Instead, he argues that its ability to create bodies makes its difference far greater—it breaks the pattern entirely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
First, read the Arabic passage that restates the Mu'tazilite objection and the author's opening rebuttal: 'We do not concede...'
Notice how the author directly challenges the opponent's premise.
2
Identify the core claim
The opponent claims the difference is 'not greater'. The author argues it is greater, because the power differs in being suitable for creating bodies.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author points out that if you disagree, that is exactly the point of dispute—so the argument is not settled by assertion.
This is a dialectical move: the burden of proof remains on the opponent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ثانياً : « تلك القدرة إذا كانت مخالفة لهذه القدر ، لم تكن مخالفتها لهذه القدر التي عندنا أشد من مخالفة بعض هذه القدر لبعض . وإذا كانت هذه القدر ، مع مخالفة بعضها البعض ، لا تصلح لخلق الجسم ، فكذلك تلك القدرة وجب أن لا تصلح لخلق الجسم . . فنقول : هذا أيضاً في غاية السقوط . وبيانه من وجوه : الأول : لا نسلم أن مخالفة تلك القدرة المفروضة ، لهذه القدر ، ليست مخالفة بعضها البعض . فإن من الجائز أن يقال : إن هذه القدر - وإن كان بعضها يخالف بعضها - إلا أنها متشاركة في أنها لا تصلح لخلق الجسم ، أما تلك القدرة المفروضة فهي مخالفة الجملة هذه القدر. من حيث إن تلك القدرة صالحة لخلق الجسم ، وجملة هذه القدر ، لا تصلح لخلق الجسم . وكانت مخالفة هذه القدرة الجملة هذه القدر ، أشد من مخالفة بعض هذه القدر لبعض . فإن ادعيتم أن الأمر ليس كذلك ، فهو عين محل النزاع. الثاني : سلمنا أن مخالفة تلك القدرة لهذه القدر ، ليست أشد من مخالفة بعض هذه القدر لبعض . إلا أن هذه القدر، لها ماهيات مخصوصة ، ولها أيضاً : أن بعضها مخالف لبعض . فعلى هذا : الامتناع ليس هو كون بعضها مخالفاً للبعض ، حتى يطرد هذا الحكم في جميع القدر ، التي يخالف بعضها بعضاً . بل علة هذا الامتناع : تلك الماهية المخصوصة . ولما كانت تلك الماهية المخصوصية ، غير حاصلة في سائر القدر : سقط ما ذكرتم . الثالث : إن هذا الكلام يلزم عليه أن يصح على ذات الله تعالى ، كل ما على كل الحوادث . فإنه يقال : ذات الله تعالى . إما أن تكون مساوية لهذه الذوات ، أو تكون مخالفة لها . فإن كان الأول ، وجب أن يصح عليه تعالى كل ما يصح على هذه الحوادث. وإن كان الثاني لم تكن مخالفة ذاته لهذه الذوات ، إلا كمخالفة بعض هذه الحوادث. وإن كان الثاني لم تكن مخالفة ذاته. لهذه الذوات ، إلا كمخالفة بعض هذه الذوات لبعض . فكما أن هذه الذوات مخالفة بعضها لبعض ، متشاركة في الحدوث والإمكان والتغير ، وجب أن تصح كل هذه الأمور على ذات الله تعالى. ولما كان هذا الكلام باطلاً ، فكذا ما ذكرتم . وبالله التوفيق فهذا حكاية عمدة المعتزلة. في أن غير الله ، لا يقدر على فعل الجسم ، وعلى فعل الحياة . وقد ظهر ضعفه وسقوطه . فثبت : أنهم لما جوزوا كون العبد موجداً لبعض الحوادث، لزمهم تجويز كونه موجداً لكلها ، ومتى جوزوا ذلك خرج المعجز عن كونه دليلاً على الصدق .
And as for his second statement: 'That power, if it differs from these powers, its difference from these powers that we have is not greater than the difference of some of these powers from others. And if these powers, despite their mutual difference, are not suitable for creating a body, then likewise that power must not be suitable for creating a body.' Then we say: This also is extremely weak. Its clarification is from aspects: The first: we do not concede that the difference of that supposed power from these powers is not a difference of some of them from others. For it is possible to say: these powers—even if some of them differ from others—are nevertheless shared in that they are not suitable for creating a body, whereas that supposed power differs from the totality of these powers, because that power is suitable for creating a body while the totality of these powers is not suitable for creating a body. And the difference of this power from the totality of these powers is greater than the difference of some of these powers from others. If you claim that the case is not so, then that is precisely the point of dispute. The second: we concede that the difference of that power from these powers is not greater than the difference of some of these powers from others. However, these powers have specific essences, and also some of them differ from others. On this basis, the prevention is not that some of them differ from others, such that this ruling applies to all powers that differ from each other. Rather, the cause of this prevention is that specific essence. Since that specific essence is not present in other powers, what you mentioned falls. The third: This statement necessarily entails that what holds for all contingencies holds for the essence of God the Exalted. For it is said: The essence of God the Exalted—either it is equal to these essences, or it differs from them. If the first, then it is necessary that everything that holds for these contingencies holds for Him the Exalted. And if the second, then the difference of His essence from these essences is only like the difference of some of these contingencies from others. For just as these essences, despite differing from each other, share in contingency, possibility, and change, it would be necessary that all these matters hold for the essence of God the Exalted. And since this statement is false, so too what you mentioned. And by God is success. So this is the epitome of the Mu'tazilites' doctrine that other than God is not capable of producing the body or producing life. And its weakness and collapse have appeared. So it is established that when they allowed the servant to be the originator of some events, it is necessary for them to allow him to be the originator of all of them, and whenever they allow that, the miracle ceases to be evidence of truthfulness.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the second Mu'tazilite objection. The opponent argued that if a power differs from other powers, and those other powers differ among themselves yet all fail to create a body, then this differing power also cannot create a body. The author gives three arguments against this: (1) The difference is actually greater because this power can create bodies while the others cannot, so the analogy fails. (2) Even if we concede the difference is not greater, the inability is due to specific essences (māhiyyāt makhṣūṣa) of those powers, not mere difference, so it does not apply to powers of different essences. (3) The opponent's reasoning would lead to absurdity when applied to God's essence: if His essence is equal to contingent essences, then all contingent properties apply to Him; if it differs, the difference is like that among contingents, so again contingent properties apply—this is false, so the opponent's argument is false as well. The author then concludes the overall refutation: the Mu'tazilites, by allowing a servant to create some accidents, must allow him to create all events, which would invalidate miracles as proofs of prophethood.

Key Terms
القدرة
المخالفة
الصلاحية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All differences between powers are equal in kind and degree.
Correction: A power that can create bodies differs fundamentally from powers that cannot, so the analogy from ordinary powers does not apply.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the first argument against the Mu'tazilite objection?
A) The power in question does not actually exist.
B) The difference of the supposed power from other powers is greater because it alone can create bodies.
C) All powers are equal in their inability to create bodies.
D) The opponent has misquoted the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The first argument establishes that a power capable of creating bodies differs more from ordinary powers than they differ among themselves, breaking the opponent's analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do I sometimes limit God's power by comparing it to my own experience, assuming that what is impossible for me is impossible for Him? How can I actively trust in His absolute ability today?
?
The ordinary powers share the inability to create—I also share that inability. Do I live with a humble awareness of my finitude, or do I pridefully act as if I can create outcomes that only God can produce?
?
The author defends the possibility of miracles against those who deny them. Do I truly expect God to work miraculously in my life, or have I become comfortable with a deistic mindset that excludes divine intervention? Will I pray for something impossible this week?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
The Second Argument: Specific Essences as Cause
Second argument: essence-based causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument, which concedes the opponent's assumption but denies the causal link.

Core Idea
Incapacity is due to specific essence, not mere difference.

Even if we grant that the difference is not greater, the author argues that the inability of ordinary powers to create bodies is not because they differ from each other, but because of their specific essences. Since the supposed power has a different essence, the prohibition does not apply.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the Arabic text from 'الثاني: سلمنا...' to 'سقط ما ذكرتم.'
Notice the concessive phrase 'sallamnā' (we concede).
2
Distinguish between difference and essence
The author points out that ordinary powers have specific essences (māhiyyāt makhṣūṣa) that prevent them from creating bodies. It is not the mere fact of difference.
This is a key conceptual move: the cause is intrinsic essence, not relational difference.
3
Apply the distinction to the case
Since the supposed power does not have that specific essence, the ruling (prevention) does not transfer to it.

Source Text

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Difference alone is the cause of causal incapacity.

According to the second argument, what is the real cause of a power's inability to create a body?
A) Its difference from other powers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used your own limitations to dismiss what Allah can do through others, forgetting that specific essences are determined by Him? How will you change this mindset today?
?
When you feel incapable of something, do you attribute it to a general weakness or do you examine the specific essence of your situation? What would it mean to trust that Allah can grant you a new essence?
?
The author argues that inability is tied to specific essences, not mere difference. Is there an area in your life where you have accepted defeat because you saw yourself as 'different from the capable' rather than looking at your actual essence? What step will you take to realign your belief with this truth?

Causality and Created Things

The Third Argument and Overall Conclusion
Third argument: reductio ad absurdum and final conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reductio ad absurdum argument and the final conclusion that the Mu'tazilite position undermines miracles.

Core Idea
The opponent's logic would force contingent properties onto God, which is absurd.

The third argument turns the opponent's reasoning against itself: if difference among essences does not prevent sharing properties, then God's essence should share contingent properties with creatures. This is clearly false, so the opponent's argument is false. The author then concludes that allowing a servant to create some events would, by the same logic, allow creation of all events, destroying the evidentiary value of miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument
Read the Arabic from 'الثالث' to 'فكذا ما ذكرتم.' Notice the application to God's essence.
This is a powerful reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand the reduction
The author applies the opponent's principle to God: if difference among essences doesn't prevent sharing properties, then God's essence must share contingent properties like contingency, possibility, change.
The absurdity is clear to any Muslim.
3
Read the conclusion
From 'وبالله التوفيق' to the end. The author summarizes that the Mu'tazilite position leads to allowing a servant to create all events, which invalidates miracles as proofs.
This ties back to prophethood and miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ثانياً : « تلك القدرة إذا كانت مخالفة لهذه القدر ، لم تكن مخالفتها لهذه القدر التي عندنا أشد من مخالفة بعض هذه القدر لبعض . وإذا كانت هذه القدر ، مع مخالفة بعضها البعض ، لا تصلح لخلق الجسم ، فكذلك تلك القدرة وجب أن لا تصلح لخلق الجسم . . فنقول : هذا أيضاً في غاية السقوط . وبيانه من وجوه : الأول : لا نسلم أن مخالفة تلك القدرة المفروضة ، لهذه القدر ، ليست مخالفة بعضها البعض . فإن من الجائز أن يقال : إن هذه القدر - وإن كان بعضها يخالف بعضها - إلا أنها متشاركة في أنها لا تصلح لخلق الجسم ، أما تلك القدرة المفروضة فهي مخالفة الجملة هذه القدر. من حيث إن تلك القدرة صالحة لخلق الجسم ، وجملة هذه القدر ، لا تصلح لخلق الجسم . وكانت مخالفة هذه القدرة الجملة هذه القدر ، أشد من مخالفة بعض هذه القدر لبعض . فإن ادعيتم أن الأمر ليس كذلك ، فهو عين محل النزاع. الثاني : سلمنا أن مخالفة تلك القدرة لهذه القدر ، ليست أشد من مخالفة بعض هذه القدر لبعض . إلا أن هذه القدر، لها ماهيات مخصوصة ، ولها أيضاً : أن بعضها مخالف لبعض . فعلى هذا : الامتناع ليس هو كون بعضها مخالفاً للبعض ، حتى يطرد هذا الحكم في جميع القدر ، التي يخالف بعضها بعضاً . بل علة هذا الامتناع : تلك الماهية المخصوصة . ولما كانت تلك الماهية المخصوصية ، غير حاصلة في سائر القدر : سقط ما ذكرتم . الثالث : إن هذا الكلام يلزم عليه أن يصح على ذات الله تعالى ، كل ما على كل الحوادث . فإنه يقال : ذات الله تعالى . إما أن تكون مساوية لهذه الذوات ، أو تكون مخالفة لها . فإن كان الأول ، وجب أن يصح عليه تعالى كل ما يصح على هذه الحوادث. وإن كان الثاني لم تكن مخالفة ذاته لهذه الذوات ، إلا كمخالفة بعض هذه الحوادث. وإن كان الثاني لم تكن مخالفة ذاته. لهذه الذوات ، إلا كمخالفة بعض هذه الذوات لبعض . فكما أن هذه الذوات مخالفة بعضها لبعض ، متشاركة في الحدوث والإمكان والتغير ، وجب أن تصح كل هذه الأمور على ذات الله تعالى. ولما كان هذا الكلام باطلاً ، فكذا ما ذكرتم . وبالله التوفيق فهذا حكاية عمدة المعتزلة. في أن غير الله ، لا يقدر على فعل الجسم ، وعلى فعل الحياة . وقد ظهر ضعفه وسقوطه . فثبت : أنهم لما جوزوا كون العبد موجداً لبعض الحوادث، لزمهم تجويز كونه موجداً لكلها ، ومتى جوزوا ذلك خرج المعجز عن كونه دليلاً على الصدق .

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the second Mu'tazilite objection. The opponent argued that if a power differs from other powers, and those other powers differ among themselves yet all fail to create a body, then this differing power also cannot create a body. The author gives three arguments against this: (1) The difference is actually greater because this power can create bodies while the others cannot, so the analogy fails. (2) Even if we concede the difference is not greater, the inability is due to specific essences (māhiyyāt makhṣūṣa) of those powers, not mere difference, so it does not apply to powers of different essences. (3) The opponent's reasoning would lead to absurdity when applied to God's essence: if His essence is equal to contingent essences, then all contingent properties apply to Him; if it differs, the difference is like that among contingents, so again contingent properties apply—this is false, so the opponent's argument is false as well. The author then concludes the overall refutation: the Mu'tazilites, by allowing a servant to create some accidents, must allow him to create all events, which would invalidate miracles as proofs of prophethood.

Key Terms
ذات الله
الحدوث
الإمكان
التغير
المعجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent's principle about powers is harmless and only applies to creatures.
Correction: It leads to attributing contingent properties to God, which is blasphemous.

Checkpoint
What is the ultimate consequence of the Mu'tazilite position as concluded by the author?
A) Miracles become impossible.
B) Servants can create all events, making miracles non-unique as proofs.
C) God becomes unable to create bodies.
D) The universe becomes eternal.

Key Takeaway
The third argument shows that the opponent's reasoning leads to absurdity regarding God's essence, and the overall conclusion ties the refutation to the crucial role of miracles in confirming prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every event in your life is directly from Allah, or do you unconsciously attribute power to your own actions and decisions? This lesson shows that even allowing a small human role in creation leads to absurdity. When you think of your accomplishments, do you sincerely affirm that Allah alone created them, or is there a hidden sense of self-sufficiency? Pray to Allah for sincerity.
?
The Mu'tazilites' error was to argue that humans create some actions. In your daily life, do you ever feel that you are the originator of your good deeds, your skills, or your success? How does this lesson's logic challenge that feeling? Commit to saying 'Allah created this' for your next achievement.
?
Miracles as proofs of prophethood lose their force if humans could produce them. When you read about the miracles of the Prophet ﷺ, do you realize that they are decisive signs from Allah alone? Or do you sometimes doubt their uniqueness because you see extraordinary events in the world? Let this lesson remind you that true creation of life and bodies is exclusive to Allah, cementing prophethood. What can you do today to strengthen your certainty in the miracles?

First proof: The servant's power is ineffective in bringing things into existence
Presenting the first proof via syllogism and the argument from possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that the servant's act is brought about only by God's power, based on the nature of possibility.

Core Idea
Possibility demands a specific cause, not an unspecified one.

The author begins the second chapter by presenting the first of several proofs that the servant's power has no effective role in creation. The proof is a simple syllogism: the servant's act is possible, and every possible is caused by God. This lesson unpacks the syllogism and the critical argument that possibility necessitates a specific cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism
Read the first few lines and identify the two premises and the conclusion of the syllogism. Notice that the first premise is uncontested.
The syllogism is: (1) The servant's act is possible. (2) Every possible occurs by God's power. Therefore, the servant's act occurs by God's power.
2
Understand the defense of the second premise
The author provides a proof for the second premise. The key concept is 'possibility' (al-imkan). Understand that possibility is a single property common to all possible things and that it entails a need for a cause.
3
Analyze the argument about unspecified cause
The author argues that possibility cannot necessitate an unspecified cause because an unspecified cause has no determinate existence in external reality. Therefore, only a specific cause can satisfy the need.
This is the core philosophical move: an indeterminate cause cannot exist externally.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The specific cause that possibility necessitates is God's power. Reflect on how this argument supports the overall claim that the servant's power is not effective in bringing things into existence.
The proof ends abruptly, but the intended conclusion is clear.
