Fifth Argument: Child's Speech Requires Knowledge of Letters
Fifth argument: showing that a child's speech requires specific knowledge of letters and speech organs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument that a child's speech requires specific knowledge of letters and speech organs, and how this challenges the idea that such actions are purely voluntary.

Core Idea
Every letter requires a specific instrument — showing speech is not instinctive but learned.

This lesson presents the fifth argument from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah, focusing on a child's speech. The argument aims to demonstrate that the production of speech necessitates comprehension of specific letters and precise control of speech organs. This raises questions about whether children's actions are purely voluntary or based on knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own logical flow come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is incomplete, ending at 'الحلق'.
2
Identify the premise about speech
The passage states that speech consists of consecutive sequential letters. This is a key premise: without letters, no speech.
3
Understand the inference about letters
Each specific letter is produced by a specific instrument (like the throat) in a specific manner. This implies detailed knowledge and control.
The argument is building towards a conclusion that the child must have knowledge of these letters and organs.
4
Reflect on the argument's purpose
Ask yourself why this argument is being presented. It opposes the idea that such actions are purely voluntary without underlying knowledge.
This connects to the broader discussion of human actions and causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إن الصبي إذا تكلم. فلا شك أنه يعقل هذه الحروف المخصوصة . وذلك لأن الكلام عبارة عن الحروف المتوالية المتعاقبة . فلو لم يكن قادراً على الإتيان بالحروف، لم يكن قادراً على الإتيان بالكلام . لكن الإتيان بكل واحد من هذه الحروف المخصوصة ، إنما يكون بآلة مخصوصة ، على كيفية مخصوصة ، ووضع تلك الآلات على وجه مخصوص ، وهي : الحلق
The fifth: Indeed the child when he speaks, there is no doubt that he comprehends ('aqala) these specific letters (huruf). That is because speech (kalam) consists of consecutive and sequential letters. So if he were not capable of producing the letters, he would not be capable of producing speech. But the production of each of these specific letters is only by a specific instrument (ala), in a specific manner (kayfiyya), and the placement of those instruments in a specific way, namely: the throat (halq)...
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage begins the fifth argument, focusing on the case of a child speaking. It states that the child must understand the specific letters because speech is composed of successive letters. Without the ability to produce letters, he could not produce speech. The production of each letter requires specific speech organs (such as the throat) arranged in a specific manner. The argument is incomplete, but it seems intended to show that such specific production requires detailed knowledge and intention beyond what a child naturally possesses, thereby raising a similar objection as before against the view that such actions are purely voluntary.

Key Terms
صبي (child)
حروف (letters)
كلام (speech)
آلة (instrument)
حلق (throat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Sounds
B) Consecutive sequential letters
C) Words
D) Meanings

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument shows that even a child's speech involves detailed knowledge of letters and speech organs, challenging purely instinctive explanations.

?

?

?

The Servant Has No Power to Create
Author presents the first argument proving the servant cannot create.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument that a human being cannot be the creator of his own existence, based on the discontinuity of power.

Core Idea
True power over existence must be constant; human power is not.

Sanusi begins his defense of divine exclusive power over creation with a logical argument from the nature of human existence. If humans had the power to create, it would have to be effective continuously, but we observe that it is not. This short passage establishes a key premise: that the servant's power is intermittent and thus cannot be the source of his own existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Try to follow the logic from premise to conclusion.
The argument is compact; read slowly.
2
Identify the two states
Notice the two states: when the servant exists (he has some power) and before existence (no power). This contrast is central.
The argument uses these two states to show inconsistency.
3
Understand the 'distinction' point
The passage says the distinction between these states should be perceived clearly (necessarily known). But there is confusion—why?
Confusion implies lack of real power over existence.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Because confusion occurs, the servant cannot be the creator of his own existence. Realize how this refutes those who claim human creative power.
The conclusion is that the servant has no power to create (al-ījād).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوده مستمر على عدمه الأصلي، فلم يكن لقدرته فيه أثر، فيمتنع كونه قادرا عليه. لأنه لما كان في إحدى الحالتين قادراً عليه، وفي الحالة الثانية لا قدرة له عليه، والتخيير بين الحال التي فيها يقدر على الفعل، وبين الحال التي لا يقدر فيها على الفعل، يجب أن يكون مدركاً. لأن التمييز بين حال الاقتدار، وبين حال عدم الاقتدار: تمييز معلوم بالضرورة. فكان يجب أن لا يحصل هذا الالتباس. وحيث حصل: علمنا أن العبد لا قدرة له على الايجاد.
His existence continues upon his original non-existence, so his power has no effect in it, thus it is impossible for him to be powerful over it. Because when he was powerful over it in one of the two states, and in the second state he has no power over it, and the distinguishing between the state in which he is able to act and the state in which he is not able to act must be perceived. For the distinction between the state of capacity and the state of incapacity is a distinction known necessarily. So it was necessary that this confusion should not occur. But since it did occur, we know that the servant has no power to create.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument against the human ability to create (al-ijiad). The author argues from the nature of the servant's existence: his existence continues upon his original non-existence. If the servant had power over his own existence, his power would have effect continuously. But we observe that in one state (when he exists) he is powerful over some acts, and in another state (before existence) he had no power. The distinction between these states must be perceived necessarily. Yet confusion occurs, indicating that the servant does not have power to create. The argument relies on the idea that true power over existence requires constant effect, which is not the case for the servant.

Key Terms
الايجاد (al-ījād: creation)
القدرة (al-qudra: power)
المدرك (al-mudrik: perception)
التمييز (al-tamyīz: distinction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the servant's ability to act implies he can create.
Correction: The argument shows that power over existence is different from power over actions within existence; the servant's power is intermittent and thus cannot be creative power.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why can the servant not be powerful over his own existence?
A) Because his power is constant and always effective.
B) Because his power varies between states, showing it is not continuous.
C) Because he is unaware of his own existence.
D) Because he cannot distinguish between states of power.

Key Takeaway
The discontinuity of human power over existence proves that only Allah is the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself believing that your own intelligence or effort is the real cause of your achievements? How does this passage's argument that your power is discontinuous and not creative challenge that feeling?
?
When you face confusion about your abilities—like why you can do something one day and not the next—does that confusion prompt you to rely more on Allah as the only constant Creator?
?
If you truly internalized that 'the servant has no power to create,' how would it change the way you pray, plan, or respond to success and failure today?

The Perplexity Argument Against Human Creation
Author argues from perplexity about where the creative act occurs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the perplexity about the locus of creation demonstrates that only Allah is the true creator.

Core Idea
Perplexity about where creation occurs proves the servant cannot be the creator.

This is the second argument in the series. It draws attention to a simple but powerful observation: if humans were truly creators, they would have clear knowledge of what exactly they create and where the effect occurs. But when we reflect, we find ourselves confused—does creation affect the essence, existence, or both? This confusion shows that we are not the real creators.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the rhetorical question format.
2
Analyze the perplexity
Understand the three possibilities presented: essence, existence, or both. Why would a true creator know which one?
3
Draw the conclusion
Grasp the logical implication: if the servant were the creator, he would know; but he doesn't, so he isn't the creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها: إن الناس تحيروا في أن من أوجد شيئاً. فتأثير إيجاده. أيحصل في نفس الماهية، أو في الوجود، أو فيهما؟ ولو كان هذا الإيجاد واقعاً به، لعلم بالضرورة: أن الذي أوجده وأوقعه: ماذا؟
Second: People are perplexed concerning the one who creates something: does the effect of his creation occur in the essence itself, or in existence, or in both? And if this creation were actually performed by him, it would be known necessarily: what is it that he created and effected?
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This is the second argument. It points out the confusion people have about the locus of the creative act: whether it affects the essence, existence, or both. If the servant were truly the creator, he would necessarily know what exactly he created and where the effect occurs. But people are perplexed, indicating that the servant does not know, and therefore he cannot be the creator.

Key Terms
الماهية (al-māhiyya: essence)
الوجود (al-wujūd: existence)
الإيجاد (al-ījād: creation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that creation is simply causing something to exist, without needing to know the details.
Correction: This argument shows that if you are the creator, you must know exactly what you are affecting—whether essence, existence, or both. Perplexity reveals lack of true creative power.

Checkpoint
What does the second argument use to show that the servant is not the creator?
A) The fact that people are confused about whether creation affects essence or existence.
B) The fact that people know exactly where creation occurs.
C) The fact that people agree on the nature of creation.
D) The fact that the effect is always in the essence.

Key Takeaway
Perplexity about the locus of creation proves that human beings cannot be the true creators, as a real creator would have necessary knowledge of his act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own achievements—your career, relationships, or skills—do you ever feel a quiet pride as if you created them? How does the perplexity argument strip that feeling away, and what must you now consciously attribute to Allah alone?
?
Have you ever acted as though you fully understood what you were doing when you 'made' something happen? This argument says a true creator knows exactly the locus of creation. Will you today, in every action, repeat 'Allah created this, not I' and mean it?
?
If you truly believed that your acts are not creation but mere acquisition, how would your gratitude change? What one thing today will you thank Allah for that you previously credited to yourself?

Third Argument: Perplexity About the Property of Effecting
Author argues from disagreement on whether the property of effecting is the same as the effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about whether the property of effecting is identical to the effect or distinct, and how this debate leads to a perplexity that undermines human agency in creation.

Core Idea
The identity of causing and effect is not self-evident, leading to irresolvable confusion.

The author presents a third argument based on the perplexity humans face when trying to define the relationship between the property of effecting and the effect itself. Two opposing views are cited, each with its own reasoning, but neither resolves the confusion. This indicates that the true nature of causation is beyond human grasp, supporting the thesis that humans are not the true creators of their actions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the disagreement
Read the passage and identify the two conflicting positions on the relationship between the property of effecting and the effect.
The disagreement is framed as a perplexity (تحير) that humans cannot resolve.
2
Examine the reasoning for each view
Analyze why some say they are distinct (based on description) and why others say they are identical (to avoid infinite regress).
Notice that each argument relies on a different logical concern.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this unresolved perplexity supports the larger argument that humans are not the true agents of creation.
The inability to settle this basic question shows our limited knowledge of causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها: إن الناس تحيروا في أن المؤثرية. هل هي نفس الأثر؟ منهم من قال: غيرها. لأن المؤثر موصوف بالمؤثرية، وغير موصوف بالأثر. فأحدهما مغاير للآخر. ومنهم من قال: بل المؤثرية نفس الأثر. إذا لو كان مغايراً له، لكان اقتضاء ذات المؤثر لتلك المؤثرية زائداً عليه. ولزم التسلسل.
Third: People are perplexed about the property of effecting: is it the same as the effect? Some say it is different, because the effecter is described with the property of effecting and not described with the effect, so one is different from the other. Others say that the property of effecting is the same as the effect, for if it were different, then the requirement of the essence of the effecter for that property of effecting would be additional to it, and infinite regress would follow.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This is the third argument. It highlights the disagreement about the relationship between the property of causing (al-mu'aththiriyya) and the effect (al-athar). Some say they are distinct because the cause is qualified by the property of causing but not by the effect. Others say they are identical, arguing that if they were distinct, the cause would require an additional property, leading to infinite regress. This unresolved confusion indicates that humans do not grasp the true nature of creation, so they cannot be its agents.

Key Terms
المؤثرية (al-mu'aththiriyya: property of effecting)
الأثر (al-athar: effect)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul: infinite regress)
الاقتضاء (al-iqtiḍā': requirement)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The property of effecting is obviously the same as the effect.
Correction: The passage shows that scholars disagree, with valid arguments on both sides, indicating the concept is not intuitively known.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of disagreement in the third argument?
A) Whether the effect is created by the cause.
B) Whether the property of effecting is the same as the effect or different.
C) Whether the effecter is described by the effect.
D) Whether infinite regress is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The unresolved debate about whether the property of effecting is identical to the effect shows that human understanding of causation is limited.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your day, do you ever pause to consider that your ability to 'cause' anything depends on a property of effecting that even scholars cannot fully define? How does this uncertainty affect your confidence in your own plans?
?
The scholars argue whether the property of effecting is the same as the effect. If you cannot be certain about the very nature of causing, how can you be certain that you—rather than Allah—are the true cause of your actions? Does this not call you to repent of any independent sense of power?
?
Imagine standing before Allah and being asked: 'Did you act as if you understood the property of effecting, or did you realize that your causation is entirely borrowed?' How would you answer based on what this lesson has shown you?

The Locus of Knowledge Argument
Author uses perplexity about knowledge's locus to deny human creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the confusion about the locus of knowledge proves that humans cannot be the creators of their own knowledge.

Core Idea
If you created your knowledge, you'd know where it lives.

This lesson presents a fascinating argument from common human experience: people disagree about where knowledge is located—is it in the heart, brain, or soul? If we had created our own knowledge, we would surely know where we put it. The fact that we are unsure shows that knowledge comes from outside us, from Allah. This argument also highlights the deeper point that most creatures cannot even conceive of the act of creation itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with a question
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, keeping in mind the question: What does the confusion about the locus of knowledge tell us?
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
Notice that the passage first discusses perplexity about where knowledge resides, then draws a conclusion about the incapacity to grasp creation.
The argument has a clear conditional: if the servant created knowledge, he would know its location.
3
Examine the conditional statement
Focus on the line: 'if the servant were the creator of knowledge, he would necessarily know in which place he created it.' Why must this be true?
A creator knows what he creates and where he places it.
4
Consider the broader implication
The author extends the argument to animals: if even humans cannot conceive of creation, how can animals be said to create?
This reinforces the idea that only Allah creates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها: إن الناس تحيروا في أن محل العلم. أهو القلب، أو الدماغ أو شيء آخر سواهما - هو النفس الناطقة؟ - وبتقدير أن كون محل العلم هو القلب أو الدماغ. فهو جميع أجزائها، أو بعض أجزائها. ولو كان موجد العلم العبد، لوجب أن يعلم أنه في أي موضع أوجده؟ وفي أي محل أحدثه؟ ولو كان الأمر كذلك، لما بقي ذلك الاشتباه. وحيث بقي، علمنا: أن حصول هذه العلوم ليس بإيجاده. فظهر: أن اختلاف الناس في هذه المطالب، يدل على أن عقول أكثر الخلق قاصرة عن حضور ماهية الإيجاد والتكوين. وإذا كان كذلك، فكيف يدعى ان جميع الحيوانات، حتى البهائم والحشرات تقصد إلى الإيجاد والتكوين والإنشاء مع أن حقيقة الإيجاد والتكوين غير متصورة البتة عندهم.
Fourth: People are perplexed about the locus of knowledge: is it the heart, the brain, or something else besides them - the rational soul? And assuming that the locus of knowledge is the heart or brain, is it all of their parts or some of them? And if the servant were the creator of knowledge, he would necessarily know in which place he created it and in which locus he originated it. And if it were so, that confusion would not remain. But since it remains, we know that the occurrence of these knowledges is not by his creation. Thus it appears: that the disagreement of people concerning these questions indicates that the minds of most creatures are incapable of grasping the quiddity of creation and bringing-into-being. And if this is so, how can it be claimed that all animals, even beasts and insects, intend to create and bring into being and originate, while the reality of creation and bringing-into-being is not at all conceived by them?
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This is the fourth argument, focusing on the locus of knowledge (al-‘ilm). People disagree about whether it resides in the heart, brain, rational soul, etc. If a person were the creator of his own knowledge, he would know exactly where he placed it. The persistent confusion proves that knowledge is not his creation. The author then draws a further conclusion: this confusion shows that most creatures cannot grasp the quiddity (mahiyya) of creation and bringing-into-being (al-takwin). Therefore, it is absurd to claim that even animals intend to create, since intention requires conception, which they lack.

Key Terms
محل العلم (maḥall al-‘ilm: locus of knowledge)
النفس الناطقة (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa: rational soul)
ماهية الإيجاد والتكوين (māhiyyat al-ījād wa al-takwīn: quiddity of creation and bringing-into-being)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the locus of knowledge is obviously the brain, and that this is well-established, so the argument is irrelevant.
Correction: The argument does not depend on the correct locus; it uses the fact of disagreement itself. Even today, neuroscientists debate how and where memories are stored. The persistent uncertainty shows we did not create our knowledge, because a creator would know exactly where he placed it.

Checkpoint
What does the confusion about the locus of knowledge indicate according to the fourth argument?
A) That knowledge is created by the servant.
B) That knowledge is not created by the servant.
C) That the servant knows exactly where knowledge resides.
D) That the brain is the locus of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The confusion about the locus of knowledge proves that knowledge is not created by humans; it must come from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to know many things, but have you ever paused to ask yourself where that knowledge actually resides? If you were truly its creator, would you not know its exact location? Yet you don't—so who placed it within you? Let that lead you to humble gratitude toward the All-Knowing.
?
The author says most creatures cannot grasp the quiddity of creation. Have you ever truly pondered the reality of your own thoughts? Do you take them for granted, or do you recognize them as gifts from the Creator? What would change if you saw every thought as a direct act of Allah?
?
You may have studied the brain or heart for years, but can you explain how a single thought arises? Why do you still not know where knowledge lives? Does this not demand that you humble yourself before the One who knows all things? Commit today to acknowledge your utter dependence on Him for even the simplest act of knowing.

Refutation of Partial Conception in Causal Intention
Author refutes objection that animals' partial conception of creation suffices for intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why intention to create requires full conception of the quiddity of creation, and why partial conception is insufficient for causal agency.

Core Idea
Intention to create requires full conception of creation's reality.

The discussion now turns to a possible objection: could animals, with a partial understanding of creation, still intend to bring something into existence? Sanusi refutes this by showing that even most humans lack any conception of the quiddity of creation, making such intention impossible. This lesson examines the logical link between conception and intention.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: an objector's claim followed by the author's refutation.
The objection is introduced by 'فإن قيل' and the answer by 'والجواب'.
2
Identify the core condition
The author states that intention (al-qasd) to attain something is conditional upon its conception. Find this line in the passage.
This is the key principle.
3
Apply the condition to animals
Since the quiddity of creation is not conceived even by most humans, it is certainly not conceived by animals. Therefore, they cannot intend to create.
The argument is by extension from humans to animals.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: it reinforces that all causal agency requires knowledge, and that animals do not have the necessary awareness to be creators.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن حقيقة الايجاد والتكوين متصورة من بعض الوجوه، عند البهائم والحشرات. وإن كان كمال تصور هذه الماهية غير حاصل عندهم. وإذا كان كذلك، كفى ذلك القدر من التصور، في إمكان قصد هذه الحيوانات، إلى الإيجاد والتكوين؟ والجواب: إن ماهية الإيجاد والتكوين غير متصورة عند الأكثرين من البشر، والأكثرين من البهائم والحشرات. وإذا كانت هذه الماهية غير متصورة عندها، استحال منها أن تقصد إليها وإلى تحصيلها. لأن القصد إلى تحصيل الشيء مشروط بتصوره وحصول الشعور بماهيئته. وبالله التوفيق.
If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that the reality of creation and bringing-into-being is conceived in some respects by beasts and insects, even if the perfect conception of this quiddity is not attained by them. And if so, that measure of conception suffices for the possibility of these animals intending to create and bring into being? The answer: The quiddity of creation and bringing-into-being is not conceived by most humans, and most beasts and insects. And since this quiddity is not conceived by them, it is impossible for them to intend it and to seek it, because intending to attain something is conditional upon its conception and the occurrence of awareness of its quiddity. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector suggests that even if most people do not fully grasp the quiddity of creation, animals might have a partial conception enough to intend it. The author refutes this by stating that even most humans do not conceive it at all, let alone animals. Intention (al-qasd) requires conception (al-tasawwur) and awareness (al-shu‘ur) of the quiddity. Since creation is not conceived, intention is impossible. The final phrase 'And with Allah is success' is a pious closure.

Key Terms
الماهية (al-māhiyya: quiddity)
التصور (al-taṣawwur: conception)
القصد (al-qaṣd: intention)
الشعور (al-shu‘ūr: awareness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Even a vague or partial idea of creation could allow an animal to intend to create something.
Correction: The author insists that intention requires actual conception of the quiddity (true essence) of creation, not just a vague notion.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the possibility that animals intend to create?
A) Because animals are not intelligent enough to intend anything.
B) Because neither humans nor animals have any conception of the quiddity of creation.
C) Because animals do not seek to create anything.
D) Because animals have full conception of creation but choose not to act.

Key Takeaway
Intention to create requires full conception of the quiddity of creation; without it, intention is impossible.

?
If most humans do not conceive the quiddity of creation, how much more careful must you be about what you intend to bring about in your life? Are you assuming you know enough about actions like repentance, patience, or gratitude to intend them properly? What step will you take this week to deepen your conception of one of these?

The Sixth Proof: From Commonality of Possibility
Ibn al-Qayyim presents a conditional proof that if the servant could create one contingent, he could create all.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that if a servant could create any contingent thing, he would have to be able to create all contingent things, which is impossible, thus proving he cannot create even one.

Core Idea
Possibility is the only relevant property for being within power, and it is shared by all contingent things.

This lesson presents the sixth proof, which uses a conditional argument to show that if a human servant could create any contingent thing, he would have to be able to create all of them. Since only Allah has omnipotence, this leads to an impossible conclusion. The author explains the logical connection step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's structure
Notice that this is a conditional argument: if A then B, B is impossible, so A is impossible. Identify A (servant creates some contingents) and B (servant creates all contingents).
The argument is called luzumiyya.
2
Understand the mulāzama (connection)
The author gives the first of several aspects explaining why creating some implies creating all. It hinges on the nature of possibility.
3
Analyze the reasoning about possibility
The argument strips away necessity and impossibility, leaving possibility as the sole factor for being within power. Since possibility is common to all contingents, power over one extends to all.
4
Confirm the conclusion
The consequent (creating all) is impossible, so the antecedent (creating some) must be false. This proves that only Allah can create.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان السادس: أن نقول: العبد لو صح منه إيجاد بعض الممكنات، لصح منه إيجاد كلها. واللازم محال، فالملزوم محال. وبيان الملازمة من وجوه: الأول: إن العبد لو قدر على إيجاد بعض الممكنات، لكان كون ذلك البعض مقدوراً إنما كان: لكونه ممكن الوجود. فإنا لو رفعنا الإمكان، بقي إما الوجوب، وإما الامتناع. وهما يحيلان المقدورية، وما يحيل المقدورية لم يفد صحة المقدورية. ولما ثبت أنه لا مدخل لهما في هذا الباب، لم يبق إلا الإمكان. والإمكان قضية مشتركة بين كل الممكنات، فلزم من هذا كون جميع الممكنات مقدورة للعبد.
The sixth proof: We say: If it were valid for the servant to create some contingent things, it would be valid for him to create all of them. The consequent is impossible, so the antecedent is impossible. The demonstration of the connection is from several aspects: The first: If the servant were able to create some contingent things, then that some being within his power would only be because it is possible of existence. For if we remove possibility, there remains either necessity or impossibility, and both render the thing incapable of being within power, and what renders capability impossible does not yield the validity of capability. And since it is established that they have no entry in this matter, nothing remains except possibility. And possibility is a common property among all contingent things, so it follows from this that all contingent things are within the power of the servant.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This is the sixth proof, structured as a conditional argument (called luzumiyya). The author asserts that if a servant could create some contingent things (al-mumkinat), then he could create all of them. The consequent is deemed impossible (because only God is omnipotent), so the antecedent must be false. The author then explains the connection (al-mulazama) with the first of several aspects: the reason a particular contingent is within power is solely its possibility (al-imkan). Removing possibility leaves necessity or impossibility, which preclude being within power. Since possibility is the only relevant factor and it is common to all contingents, power over one contingent necessarily implies power over all. This leads to an impossible consequence, proving that the servant cannot create even one thing.

Key Terms
البرهان (al-burhān: proof)
الممكنات (al-mumkināt: contingent things)
الإمكان (al-imkān: possibility)
الوجوب (al-wujūb: necessity)
الامتناع (al-imtinā‘: impossibility)
المقدورية (al-maqdūriyya: being within power)
الملازمة (al-mulāzama: connection/implication)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that power over some contingents is based on their specific properties, but this argument shows that only possibility matters, which is universal.
Correction: The proof argues that the only reason a contingent is within power is its possibility, which is shared by all contingents, so specific properties are irrelevant.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth proof, what is the common property that would allow the servant to create all contingent things if he could create one?
A) Necessity
B) Impossibility
C) Possibility
D) Existence

Key Takeaway
The sixth proof uses the commonality of possibility to show that if a servant could create any contingent, he could create all, which is impossible, so he cannot create even one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that you cannot create even the smallest thing, would you still feel pride in your accomplishments? How does this proof challenge your sense of self-sufficiency?
?
Consider a time you felt you were the cause of something good in your life. After learning that only God can create, how should you respond when you are praised for your efforts?
?
This proof shows that possibility is the only relevant factor for being within power. Do you ever act as if your own ability is the source of your success, rather than the fact that God made that thing possible for you? What concrete change can you make today to acknowledge His sole power?

Do Animals Conceive of Creation? Razi's Objection and Answer
Razi presents and refutes an objection about animals' intention to create.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that animals cannot intend to create because they lack even partial conception of the essence of bringing into existence.

Core Idea
Intention to create requires full conception; animals lack it.

Razi addresses a subtle objection: perhaps animals have enough conception to intend creation. He argues that even most humans lack this conception, so animals certainly do. This lesson unpacks the objection and its decisive answer, clarifying a key principle about the relationship between knowledge and agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the hypothetical question about animals' partial conception. Notice how the objection sets up a possibility.
The objection uses 'لم لا يجوز' to question why it is not permissible.
2
Analyze the answer and condition
Identify Razi's answer: the essence is not conceived by most humans or animals. Then note the logical condition: intention requires prior conception. This is the core refutation.
The phrase 'القصد إلى تحصيل الشيء مشروط بتصوره' is the key principle.
3
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider that if even humans lack full conception, then created beings cannot be sources of creation. This reinforces divine exclusivity in bringing things into existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن حقيقة الإيجاد والتكوين متصورة من بعض الوجوه ، عند البهائم والحشرات . وإن كان كمال تصور هذه الماهية غير حاصل عندهم . وإذا كان كذلك ، كفى ذلك القدر من التصور ، في إمكان قصد هذه الحيوانات ، إلى الإيجاد والتكوين ؟ . والجواب : إن ماهية الإيجاد والتكوين غير متصورة عند الأكثرين من البشر ، والأكثرين من البهائم والحشرات . وإذا كانت هذه الماهية غير متصورة عندها ، استحال منها أن تقصد إليها وإلى تحصيلها . لأن القصد إلى تحصيل الشيء مشروط بتصوره وحصول الشعور بماهيته : وبالله التوفيق
For if it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that the reality of bringing into existence and originating is conceived in some respects among beasts and insects, even if complete conception of this essence is not obtained for them? And if that is the case, that degree of conception suffices for the possibility of these animals intending to bring into existence and originate? The answer: The essence of bringing into existence and originating is not conceived among most humans and most beasts and insects. And if this essence is not conceived for them, it is impossible for them to intend it and to obtain it, because the intention to obtain a thing is conditioned by its conception and the attainment of awareness of its essence. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector proposes that animals might have a partial conception of the essence of bringing into existence and originating, and that this partial conception might be enough for them to intend to create. The answer denies that animals have any conception of this essence, stating that even most humans lack it. Since intention to obtain something requires prior conceptualization, and animals lack even partial conceptualization, they cannot intend to create. This refutes any claim that created beings can engage in creation.

تكوين
قصد
تصور

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Partial knowledge of creation is sufficient for intending to create.
Correction: Razi argues that intention requires full conceptualization of the essence; animals lack even partial conception, so they cannot intend.

Checkpoint

B) They might have partial conception of creation.
C) They instinctively create.
D) They are powerful beings.

Key Takeaway
Animals cannot intend to create because they lack any conception of the essence of bringing into existence.

?
If intention requires conception, then what does your intention to worship or obey God presuppose about your conception of Him? This lesson demands that you examine whether your intentions are based on a clear understanding of what you are doing. Take a moment to reflect: in your daily acts of worship, do you truly conceive of the act you intend, or are you merely going through motions?

First Face: Possibility as Shared Attribute
Razi presents the first argument that if the slave could create some, he could create all.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi argues that if a slave could create any possible thing, then he could create all possible things, based on possibility as a universal attribute.

Core Idea
Possibility is the gateway — if it allows one, it allows all.

This passage presents a powerful logical argument from Imam al-Razi. He begins with a conditional: if a created being could bring into existence some possible things, then he could bring into existence all possible things. The argument then zeroes in on why the ability to create any single possible thing would imply the ability to create all of them. The key insight lies in the nature of possibility itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the opening conditional claim: 'If the slave could validly bring into existence some possibles, then he could validly bring into existence all of them.' Note that the consequent is deemed impossible, so the antecedent must be false.
2
Analyze the entailment explanation
Razi explains the entailment from several faces; focus on the first face. Understand why the ability to create some possibles would lead to the ability to create all.
3
Identify the three rational categories
Notice how Razi removes necessity and impossibility, leaving only possibility as the grounds for being within power. Understand why necessity and impossibility preclude being within power.
4
Grasp the universal implication
Since possibility is a common judgment among all possibles, if one possible is creatable on that basis, then all are creatable. This leads to the absurd conclusion.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument challenges the Ash'ari view that human power is limited. The argument uses a reductio to show that if humans could create anything, they could create everything.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان السادس
أن نقول : العبد لو صح منه إيجاد بعض الممكنات ، لصح منه إيجاد كلها . واللازم محال ، فالملزوم محال
وبيان الملازمة من وجوه :
·
الأول : إن العبد لو قدر على إيجاد بعض الممكنات ، لكان ذلك البعض مقدوراً . إنما كان : لكونه ممكن الوجود . فإنا لو رفعنا الإمكان ، بقي إما الوجوب، وإما الامتناع . وهما يحيلان المقدورية ، وما يحيل المقدورية لم يفد صحة المقدورية . ولما ثبت أنه لا مدخل لهما في هذا الباب ، لم يبق إلا الإمكان . والإمكان قضية مشتركة بين كل الممكنات ، فلزم من هذا كون جميع الممكنات مقدورة للعبد .
The sixth proof: We say: If the slave could validly bring into existence some possibles, then he could validly bring into existence all of them. The consequent is impossible, so the antecedent is impossible. And the explanation of the entailment is from several aspects: First: If the slave had power to bring into existence some possibles, then that part would be within his power. It would only be such because of its being possible of existence. For if we remove possibility, there remains either necessity or impossibility. And both render [something] invalid for being within power, and what renders something invalid for being within power does not benefit the soundness of being within power. And since it is established that neither of them has any entry in this matter, nothing remains except possibility. And possibility is a common judgment among all possibles, so from this it follows that all possibles are within the power of the slave.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is the first face of the sixth proof. It begins by stating the proof: if the slave could create some possibles, he could create all; the consequent is impossible, so the antecedent is false. Then it explains the entailment. The first face argues that if the slave can create some possibles, the reason for their being creatable is their possibility (imkan). Necessity and impossibility preclude being creatable. Since possibility is a universal attribute of all possibles, if one possible is creatable on that basis, then all possibles are creatable. Hence the slave would be able to create all, which is absurd.

Key Terms
إمكان
وجوب
امتناع
مقدورية
ممكن الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility alone is sufficient for something to be within a creature's power without divine permission.
Correction: The argument assumes possibility as the only relevant attribute, but in Islamic theology, divine will and permission are also necessary. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum used by Razi to show that if possibility alone were sufficient, it would lead to an absurd conclusion.

Checkpoint
What does the first face identify as the common attribute that would allow the slave to create all possibles?
A) Existence
B) Necessity
C) Possibility
D) Impossibility

Key Takeaway
Razi's first face argues that if a slave could create any possible thing solely because of its possibility, then he could create all possible things—an impossibility that refutes the original claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own accomplishments—your career, skills, or even your good deeds—do you secretly believe you 'created' them yourself? Would your heart tremble if you truly accepted that no act of yours brings anything into existence except by Allah’s creation?
?
If Razi’s proof is sound, then you have zero creative power. How does that truth sit with your instinct to take credit? Can you name a specific area of your life where you habitually attribute causality to yourself, and commit today to mentally replace that with 'Allah created it'?
?
The proof demands that possibility alone is not enough for something to exist—it requires a creator. When you face a seemingly impossible situation, do you still believe it is possible for Allah to create a way out? Or do you limit His power by the laws of nature you observe?

Causality and Created Things

Second Face of the Sixth Proof: Effect of Power Is Only Existence
Razi argues that the slave's power, if any, would only grant existence, making all possibles alike.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that if a created being had creative power, it could only bestow existence, leading to the unacceptable conclusion that it could create everything.

Core Idea
Creative power can only bestow existence, making all acts of creation equivalent and thus impossible for limited beings.

In this second face of the sixth proof, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi uses a subtle argument about the relationship between existence and essence. He shows that if a slave could bring something into existence, his power would only affect the existence, not the essence. This leads to a surprising consequence: if he could create one thing, he could create everything. Such a conclusion is absurd, proving that only Allah has the power to create.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice the logical structure.
The argument uses technical terms like existence (wujud) and essence (mahiyya).
2
Understand the existence-essence distinction
Razi states that existence is additional to essence for possibles. This is a key premise. Recognize that the effect of power cannot be on the essence itself.
This premise is established in his other books.
3
Follow the priority argument (two ranks)
Razi argues that the effect of power is posterior to the determination of the essence by two ranks, because the possibility of existence precedes the effect. If power affected the essence, there would be a contradiction.
The phrase 'two ranks' is crucial.
4
Derive the conclusion about the effect
Therefore, the effect of power is only to bestow existence, not to make the essence what it is.
This is the immediate conclusion.
5
Consider the consequence for all possibles
Since existence is a single concept, power over one existence implies power over all existences, leading to the absurdity that a slave could create everything.
This reinforces the argument that only God has true creative power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إنه لو قدر العبد على إيجاد بعض الممكنات ، لما كان لقدرته تأثير إلا في إعطاء الوجود . لأنا بينا في سائر كتبنا بالدلائل الكثيرة : أن وجود الممكنات زائد على ماهياتها . ولا يمكن أن يكون تأثير القادر في تكوين الماهيات . فإن الشيء ما لم يكن ممكن الوجود ، لم يتصور أن يكون للقادر فيه تأثير . فتأثير القادر فيه مسبوق بكونه في نفسه ممكن الوجود ، وكونه في نفسه ممكن الوجود يقرر ماهيته المخصوصة . لأن إمكانه صفة له ، وصفة الشيء متأخرة بالرتبة عن الموصوف . فثبت : أن تأثير القادر فيه متأخر عن تقرر ماهيته بمرتبتين . ولو كان تأثير القادر في ماهيته ، لكان تقرر ماهيته متأخراً عن تأثير القادر فيه ، وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون المتقدم على الشيء بمرتبتين متأخراً عنه . وذلك محال . فثبت بهذا الوجه : أن تأثير القادر ليس في جعل الماهية ماهية ، بل ذلك التأثير في جعلها موجودة . فثبت : أن العبد لو كان قادراً على الإيجاد ، لما كان لقدرته تأثير إلا في إعطاء الوجود . واعلم : أن هذا كلام حسن قوي . إلا أنه يشم منه رائحة : أن المعدوم شيء . فثبت : أن العبد لو كان قادراً على إيجاد بعض الممكنات ، لما كان لقدرته تأثير إلا في إعطاء الوجود . فنقول : لو كان الأمر كذلك ، لوجب أن يقدر على كل الممكنات . وذلك لأنه لما كان لا تأثير للموجد إلا في إعطاء الوجود ، وثبت : أن الوجود قضية واحدة في جميع الممكنات ، ولا تفاوت بينهما البتة في هذا المفهوم : لزم أن يكون القادر على إيجاد بعض الممكنات قادر على إيجاد كلها . ضرورة أن القادر على الشيء قادر على مثله
Second: If the slave had power to bring into existence some possibles, his power would have no effect except in bestowing existence. For we have shown in our other books with many proofs that the existence of possibles is additional to their essences. And it is impossible for the effect of the powerful agent to be in the origination of essences. For a thing, as long as it is not possible of existence, it is not conceivable that the powerful agent have any effect in it. So the effect of the powerful agent in it is preceded by its being in itself possible of existence, and its being in itself possible of existence determines its specific essence. Because its possibility is an attribute of it, and the attribute of a thing is posterior in rank to the described. So it is established that the effect of the powerful agent in it is posterior to the determination of its essence by two ranks. And if the effect of the powerful agent were in its essence, then the determination of its essence would be posterior to the effect of the powerful agent in it, and then it would follow that what is prior to a thing by two ranks is posterior to it. That is impossible. So it is established by this aspect that the effect of the powerful agent is not in making the essence an essence, but rather that effect is in making it existent. So it is established that if the slave were powerful over bringing into existence, his power would have no effect except in bestowing existence. And know: This is good strong speech, except that it smells of the odor that the nonexistent is a thing. So it is established that if the slave were powerful over bringing into existence some possibles, his power would have no effect except in bestowing existence. So we say: If the matter were so, then he would necessarily have power over all possibles. That is because since the effect of the one who brings into existence is nothing but bestowing existence, and it is established that existence is a single concept in all possibles, and there is no difference at all between them in this concept, it follows that the one who is powerful over bringing into existence some possibles is powerful over bringing into existence all of them, necessarily because the one who is powerful over a thing is powerful over its like.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is the second face of the sixth proof. It argues that if the slave could create some possibles, his power would only affect the bestowal of existence, not the essence. The reasoning: existence is additional to essence; a powerful agent's effect cannot be on the essence because the essence is already determined by its possibility. If the agent affected the essence, a priority contradiction would arise. Therefore, the effect is only in making the essence existent. Existence is a single concept common to all possibles. Since the agent can bestow existence on some, he can do so on all, as power over something includes power over its like. This entails the absurdity that the slave can create all possibles.

Key Terms
وجود
ماهية
تأثير
ممكن الوجود
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that the nonexistent is a thing (that essences have reality before existence).
Correction: Razi acknowledges this odor but does not fully address it; the argument's strength depends on that metaphysical commitment.

Checkpoint
According to the second face, what is the only effect of power if it were to create?
A) Creating the essence
B) Determining possibility
C) Bestowing existence
D) Making the thing necessary

Key Takeaway
If the slave could create, his power could only bestow existence, and since existence is uniform, he could create all possibles – an absurd consequence proving power belongs only to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking that you can create something—like an outcome, a reputation, or even a moment of happiness—by your own power? How does Razi's argument that creative power only bestows existence, and that existence is uniform, challenge that assumption? What would it look like to genuinely attribute all creation to Allah in your daily life?
?
If existence is a single concept and only Allah can bestow it, what does that mean for how you view the things you think you 'bring about' through your actions? Are you tempted to see your efforts as independent causes rather than as occasions for Allah's creation? How can you train your heart to see Allah as the sole Creator in every moment?

Proof That Human Power Is Not Effective in Creating Actions
Author argues both divisions of power's effect are false, concluding power is not effective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical proof that human power (قدرة) cannot be the effective cause of actions because both possible accounts—with or without the substrate's participation—lead to contradictions.

Core Idea
Human power cannot independently create actions; the proof uses the incompatibility of potentiality and necessity.

This passage presents a rigorous philosophical argument that human power is not the effective cause of actions. The author, following the tradition of the philosophers (الحكماء), examines two possible ways power could be effective—with or without the body's participation—and shows both are impossible. By working through this proof, you will see why Ash'ari theology denies that human power creates actions and instead affirms that all effective causation belongs to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Let the structure of the argument sink in before analyzing.
Notice the author's use of 'impossible' (محال) to mark each refutation.
2
Identify the two divisions
The author first states both divisions are false, then argues each separately. The first division: power's effect with participation of the substrate. The second: without participation. Identify where each is discussed.
The substrate (محل) is the body or locus that receives the action.
3
Understand the contradictions
For the first division, note why making the substrate part of the effective cause is impossible: the substrate's nature (specific potentiality) contradicts the necessity of creation. For the second, trace the implication that independence in effect would require independence in existence, which is false because power depends on the substrate for existence.
These are the core logical moves; ensure you grasp the incompatibility of إمكان خاص and وجوب.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسمان باطلان ، فالقول بأن قدرة العبد مؤثرة في حدوث الفعل : محال . إنما قلنا : إنه ممتنع أن يحصل ذلك التأثير بتركه من ذلك المحل لأن ذلك المحل ليس له إلا كونه قابلاً للصفات أعني : أنه لا يمتنع حصول هذه الصفات فيه ، ولا يمتنع لا حصولها أيضاً فيه . فلو جعلنا المحل جزءاً من المؤثر، لكنا قد جعلنا القابل جزءاً من الموجد . وذلك محال . لأن هذه القابلية طبيعتها طبيعة الإمكان الخاص . والموجدية طبيعتها طبيعة الوجوب . والإمكان الخاص ينافي الوجوب . وكون المنافي جزء من المنافي الآخر محال في العقول . وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يكون تأثير القدرة في حدوث ذلك الفعل ، لا شركة من ذلك المحل . وذلك لأن وجود الشيء جزء من كونه موجداً لغيره فلو كانت هذه القدرة غنية في مؤثريتها عن ذلك المحل ، لكانت غنية أيضاً في وجودها عن ذلك المحل. لأنه متى كان المركب غنياً عن شيء ، كان كل واحد من بسيطته أيضاً غنياً عنه . ثبت : أن هذه القدرة لو كانت في مؤثريتها في الفعل غنية عن ذلك المحل ، لكانت في وجودها أيضاً غنية عن ذلك المحل . وذلك محال . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن القدرة لو كانت مؤثرة في وجود الفعل، لكان ذلك التأثير إما أن يكون بشركة من ذلك المحل ، أو لا بشركة من ذلك المحل ، وثبت كون كل واحد من هذين القسمين باطلاً . فثبت : أن القدرة غير مؤثرة في حدوث الفعل وهذا هو دليل الحكماء، على أن القوى الجسمانية غير مؤثرة في الوجود اصلا . وبالله التوفيق
Both divisions are false. So the statement that the servant's power is effective in causing the action is impossible. We say: it is impossible for that effect to occur by leaving [the substrate] from that substrate, because that substrate only has the property of being receptive to attributes, meaning: it is not impossible for these attributes to occur in it, nor is it impossible for them not to occur in it. So if we made the substrate part of the effective cause, we would have made the receiver part of the creator. And that is impossible. Because this receptivity has a nature of specific potentiality, and the creative power has a nature of necessity. Specific potentiality is incompatible with necessity. And the incompatibility of one part of the opposite with the other part is impossible in intellects. And we say: it is impossible for the power's effect in the occurrence of that action to be without participation of that substrate. Because the existence of a thing is part of its being a creator of another. So if this power were independent in its effect from that substrate, it would also be independent in its existence from that substrate. Because whenever a composite is independent of something, each of its simples is also independent of it. It is established: if this power were independent in its effect on the action from that substrate, it would also be independent in its existence from that substrate. And that is impossible. So it is established by what we mentioned: if power were effective in the existence of the action, then that effect would either be with participation of that substrate or without participation of that substrate. And it is established that both of these divisions are false. So it is established: power is not effective in the occurrence of the action. And this is the proof of the philosophers that corporeal powers are not effective in existence at all. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a proof that human power (قدرة العبد) is not effective in causing actions. The author first states that both possible divisions (i.e., power's effect either with or without the substrate's participation) are false, and then explains why. The substrate (المحل) is the body or locus that receives the action. The argument proceeds: if power were effective, its effect would either involve the substrate as a partner or not. If it involves the substrate, then the substrate—which is merely receptive (قابل)—would be part of the creative cause (موجد), but receptivity has the nature of specific potentiality (إمكان خاص) while creative power has the nature of necessity (وجوب). These are incompatible, so the substrate cannot be part of the creator. If the effect is without the substrate's participation, then the power would be independent of the substrate in its effect. But since the existence of a thing is part of its being a creator of another, independence in effect would entail independence in existence. However, the power inheres in the substrate and depends on it for existence; hence it cannot be independent. Thus both divisions are impossible, so power is not effective. The author notes that this is the proof of the philosophers (الحكماء) that bodily powers (قوى جسمانية) are not effective in bringing about existence at all.

Key Terms
قدرة
محل
إمكان خاص
وجوب
قوى جسمانية
مؤثر
موجد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof means humans have no role in actions whatsoever, denying human responsibility.
Correction: The proof only denies that human power independently creates actions (i.e., is the effective cause of their existence). It does not negate human acquisition (kasb) or moral responsibility. In Ash'ari theology, actions are created by Allah but acquired by humans, meaning humans are responsible for actions they intend and perform, even though the power to do so is not the creator of the action.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it impossible for the substrate (المحل) to be part of the effective cause (المؤثر)?
A) Because the substrate is not receptive to attributes.
B) Because the substrate's nature (specific potentiality) is incompatible with the necessity of the creator.
C) Because the power is independent in its effect from the substrate.
D) Because the substrate is unnecessary for the action.

Key Takeaway
Human power does not create actions; it is only a locus of acquisition, not the effective cause of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you take pride in an achievement, do you sincerely believe that your power alone produced it, or do you see it as a gift from Allah that He chose to create through your locus? How will you remind yourself tomorrow that your power is not the real creator, and what specific practice (e.g., saying 'Masha Allah' or making du'a) will you adopt to acknowledge this truth?
?
Have you ever felt so helpless that you despaired? How does knowing that your power is merely receptive—not effective—change your response when your efforts fall short? What is one situation right now where you can consciously surrender the outcome to Allah, trusting that He is the only creator of actions?
?
The proof argues that if your power were independent in effect, it would also be independent in existence. Is there an area of your life where you act as though you exist independently of Allah—making decisions, building relationships, or earning wealth as if you are the ultimate cause? Spend a quiet moment repenting of that mindset and commit to reciting 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' daily to root it out.

The Second Proof: Necessary Implications of Prior Sciences and Ignorances
Second proof: necessary entailment of prior sciences and ignorances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the necessary entailment of prior knowledge and ignorance to their results leads to the conclusion that all is from God.

Core Idea
Necessary entailment implies divine origin, not human choice.

This lesson presents the second proof in a series of arguments for the createdness of actions. The argument focuses on the relationship between prior knowledge and resulting knowledge, and prior ignorance and resulting ignorance, arguing that these entailments are necessary. Once established, it follows that the entire chain is from God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the parallel structure between sciences and ignorances.
2
Identify the two parallel entailments
Notice that the proof mentions both sciences and ignorances. Each has a prior state and a resulting state, and the entailment is said to be necessary.
3
Understand necessary entailment
Necessary entailment means the relationship is not contingent on human choice. It is a fixed causal relation.
This is key to the argument that the source is divine.
4
Examine the conclusion
Because the entailments are necessary, they cannot be attributed to human will. Therefore, the entire chain is from God.
The phrase 'على هذا التقدير' indicates the logical conclusion based on the premise.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this proof fits into the broader argument for divine action. It shows that knowledge and ignorance are ultimately created by God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إن استلزام تلك العلوم المتقدمة لتلك العلوم التي هي النتائج يجب أن تكون ضرورية. وكذلك استلزام تلك الجهالات المتقدمة لتلك الجهالات التي هي النتائج ، يجب أن تكون ضرورية . وعلى هذا التقدير يكون الكل من الله تعالى
And the second [proof]: that the entailment of those prior sciences for those sciences which are the results must be necessary. Likewise, the entailment of those prior ignorances for those ignorances which are the results must be necessary. And on this assumption, all is from God, exalted.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof in a series of arguments for the createdness of actions. The proof argues that the relationship between prior knowledge and resulting knowledge, as well as prior ignorance and resulting ignorance, must be a necessary entailment (istilzam daruri). Because this entailment is necessary, it cannot be attributed to human choice; rather, it must be from God. The argument follows a pattern: if the implications are not contingent on human will, then the whole chain of knowledge and ignorance is divinely created. The author states that under this assumption (taqdir), everything is from God.

Correction: The proof is only about the origin of knowledge and ignorance, not about moral accountability. Human responsibility is not addressed here.

Key Takeaway
The necessary entailment of prior knowledge and ignorance to their results indicates that the entire chain is divinely created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How will you respond to your next moment of confusion?
?

?
If the entailment of prior ignorance to resulting ignorance is necessary, then your sins and mistakes are not random; they are part of a divine order. How does this change the way you repent? Can you accept that your errors were decreed, and yet still strive to change?

The Fourth Proof: Ignorance as an External Act
The fourth proof and its defense against an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof for divine creation of actions, which argues that ignorance occurring contrary to human intention must be caused by God.

Core Idea
Ignorance stems from God, not from human choice.

In this passage, the author presents a logical proof for divine causality. He observes that humans always intend knowledge, yet ignorance occurs. Since this outcome contradicts human will, its cause must be external — ultimately, God. The passage also anticipates and refutes a possible objection, strengthening the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the first part of the passage where the author states the fourth proof clearly. Note the premises: man intends knowledge, ignorance occurs, therefore it is from another.
This is the core argument.
2
Understand the objection
Read the objection: someone might say that the person chose ignorance because they mistook it for knowledge. This challenges the initial assumption that ignorance is always against intention.
The objection introduces a nuance.
3
Follow the rebuttal
The author rebuts by pointing out that such a mistake presupposes a prior ignorance. This chain must trace back to a first ignorance created by God.
This refutes the objection by showing an infinite regress.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this proof establishes that ignorance, like other actions, is ultimately created by God. This reinforces the doctrine of divine omnipotence.
Connects to broader theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الرابع
إن الانسان لا يقصد إلا تحصيل العلم . فلما حصل الجهل ، كان حصوله على خلاف قصده واختياره. فوجب أن يكون من غيره .
فإن قالوا : ذلك لأنه اشتبه عليه هذا الجهل بالعلم . فتقول : فهو إنما اختار ذلك الجهل ، . لتقدم جهل آخر . ولا بد وأن تنتهي تلك الجهالات إلى الجهل الأول ، الواقع بخلق الله .
The fourth proof: Man intends nothing but the acquisition of knowledge. So when ignorance occurs, its occurrence is contrary to his intention and choice. So it must be from other than him. If they say: that is because he confused this ignorance with knowledge. We say: he only chose that ignorance due to a preceding ignorance. And those ignorances must end at the first ignorance, which occurs by the creation of God.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage contains the fourth proof for the creation of actions. The argument is: Man always intends to acquire knowledge. When ignorance happens, it is against his intention and choice. Therefore, ignorance must come from an external agent, i.e., God. The author then anticipates an objection: one might say that the person chose ignorance mistakenly, thinking it was knowledge. The answer is that such a mistake itself stems from a prior ignorance. This chain of ignorances must trace back to an original ignorance that is directly created by God. Thus, all ignorance is ultimately from God. The proof uses the principle that whatever occurs contrary to intention and choice must have its cause in another.

Key Terms
قصد
اختيار
جهل
خلق الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ignorance is simply a lack of knowledge and not a created entity.
Correction: The proof treats ignorance as an occurrent state that requires a cause, ultimately from God.

Checkpoint

A) Because man always intends ignorance
B) Because ignorance occurs against man's intention and choice
C) Because man has no control over his mind
D) Because ignorance is a disease

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof demonstrates that ignorance, as an event contrary to human intention, must be caused by God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself in ignorance of a truth you should know, do you immediately blame yourself — or do you stop and ask God why He has placed that ignorance upon you? What would change in your heart if you truly believed it is from Him?
?
Consider a time you insisted you were right, only to later realize you were ignorant. In that moment, could you see that your confusion itself was a veil placed by God? How does that recognition transform your repentance?
?
The proof says your intention is always knowledge — yet ignorance occurs. Is there an area of your life where you keep intending to learn but remain stuck? Will you today cry out to the Creator of all ignorance to lift that veil?

Transmitted proofs cannot prove that the servant is not creator of actions
Al-Razi begins the second investigation: transmitted proofs cannot show that the servant does not create his own acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi argues that transmitted proofs cannot be used to prove that the servant does not create his own actions, and you will learn the Mu'tazila's claim and al-Razi's initial critique.

Core Idea
Transmitted proofs are insufficient to deny human creative agency.

Al-Razi sets the stage for a critical investigation. Even if we allow verbal proofs in theological matters, he argues that transmitted proofs (دلائل سمعية) cannot prove that the servant is not the creator of his own actions. He then engages with the Mu'tazila, who claim that denying human creative agency would undermine the proofs for God, prophethood, and the Qur'an. This lesson presents al-Razi's framing and the beginning of his response.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's thesis
Read the first sentence: he says even if verbal proofs are allowed, transmitted proofs cannot prove the servant is not a creator. This is his main claim.
Notice the careful framing: he grants the permissibility of verbal proofs for the sake of argument.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's claim
The Mu'tazila argue that denying the servant's creative agency leads to three impossibilities: proving God, proving prophethood, and establishing the Qur'an as proof. Find these in the text.
These are serious consequences; al-Razi will later critique them.
3
Trace the Mu'tazila's first argument
Their first argument connects our actions' need for us to the world's need for a Maker. Understand the analogy they draw.
This is the foundation of their proof for God's existence.
4
Catch al-Razi's initial critique
Al-Razi begins his refutation by stating this argument is weak from two aspects. The first is that he does not accept the validity of the proof. This is just the start; the passage cuts off.
The full critique is not in this passage but will be continued.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني في بيان أن بتقدير أن يكون التمسك بالدلائل اللفظية في أمثال هذه المطالب جائز . إلا أنا نقول : التمسك بالدلائل السمعية في إثبات أن العبد غير موجد لأفعال نفسه : غير جائز . واعلم : أن المعتزلة قد أطنبوا في تقرير هذا المقام . وأنا أنقل حاصل ما ذكروه ، وأضم إليه من عندي : وجوهاً أخرى . أقوى وأكمل مما ذكروه ، ليكون البحث موصلا إلى أقصى الغايات ، وأكمل النهايات . قالوا : إن كل من نفى كون العبد موجداً ، فإنه يتعذر عليه إثبات الصانع ، ويتعذر عليه إثبات النبوة ، ويتعذر عليه القول بأن القرآن حجة . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، ثبت : أن كل من نفى كون العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه فإنه يتعذر عليه الاستدلال بالدلائل السمعية ، على تصحيح المطلوب . أما بيان أن كل من نفى كون العبد موجداً ، فإنه يتعذر عليه القول بإثبات الصانع . فتقريره من وجوه ثلاثة . الأول : وهو الذي ذكره المعتزلة . قالوا : إن طريقنا إلى إثبات الصانع . هو أن نقول : أفعالنا إنما افتقرت إلينا ، بسبب حدوثها . فإذا كان العالم محدثاً ، وجب افتقاره إلى الفاعل . ومعلوم أن بناء هذا الدليل : على افتقار أفعالنا إلينا. فإذا لم نعتقد هذا الأصل ، فقد انسد علينا طريق إثبات الصانع . هذا حاصل كلامهم . وهذا ضعيف من وجهين : الأول : إنا لا نسلم أن هذا الذي ذكرتم : دليل صحيح في إثبات
The second investigation: explaining that, granting that clinging to verbal proofs in such matters is permissible, we say: clinging to transmitted proofs (دلائل سمعية) to prove that the servant is not a creator of his own actions is not permissible. And know that the Mu'tazila have expanded much in establishing this position. I will transmit the gist of what they mentioned, and add to it from myself other aspects, stronger and more complete than what they mentioned, so that the investigation reaches the utmost ends and most complete finalities. They said: Whoever denies that the servant is a creator, then it is impossible for him to prove the existence of the Maker, impossible for him to prove prophethood, and impossible for him to say that the Qur'an is a proof. And if that is the case, it is established: that whoever denies that the servant is a creator of his own actions, it is impossible for him to make inferences from transmitted proofs to validate the desired conclusion. As for the explanation that whoever denies that the servant is a creator, it is impossible for him to assert the proof of the Maker, its exposition is from three aspects. The first: which the Mu'tazila mentioned. They said: our way to prove the Maker is to say: our actions required us because of their originatedness (حدوث). So if the world is originated, it is necessary for it to need an agent. And it is known that the construction of this proof is upon the need of our actions for us. So if we do not believe this principle, the way to prove the Maker is blocked for us. This is the gist of their speech. And this is weak from two aspects: the first: we do not accept that what you have mentioned is a valid proof for establishing...
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage introduces the second major investigation. Al-Razi begins by saying that even if one grants the permissibility of using verbal proofs for such matters, the use of transmitted proofs (دلائل سمعية) to show that the servant does not create his own actions is not permissible. He notes that the Mu'tazila have extensively argued this. He promises to present their arguments and then add stronger ones of his own. The Mu'tazila claim that anyone who denies that the servant is a creator will be unable to prove the existence of God, prophethood, and the authority of the Qur'an. This is because their proof for God depends on our actions requiring us; if we deny that our actions require us as their creators, then the cosmological argument collapses. Al-Razi then states that this Mu'tazila argument is weak for two reasons, and he begins the first: he does not accept that this is a valid proof for establishing the Maker.

Key Terms
دلائل سمعية
موجد
إثبات الصانع
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Razi is rejecting all transmitted proofs outright.
Correction: He is only rejecting their use for this specific claim and is critiquing the Mu'tazila's argument.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila as cited by al-Razi, what becomes impossible for one who denies that the servant is the creator of his own actions?
A) Proving the existence of the Maker
B) Proving prophethood
C) Considering the Qur'an as a proof
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi opens by rejecting the use of transmitted proofs to deny human creative agency, and he sets up the Mu'tazila's argument as a target for critique.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend a belief about God, do you lean more on transmitted proofs (like hadith) or rational arguments? How does al-Razi's warning challenge your default approach?
?
If the rational proof for God's existence depended on something you deny, would your faith remain secure? What does that reveal about the foundation of your certainty?
?
The Mu'tazila assumed their theological framework was necessary to prove God. Have you ever assumed that your own understanding is the only way to maintain belief? Could you accept another path without losing conviction?
